The Necessity of Teachers in Islamic Learning
Author argues for teacher-based learning via four authoritative quotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional Islamic emphasis on receiving knowledge directly from qualified teachers and the criticisms of relying solely on books.

Core Idea
Knowledge without a teacher is like a ship without a captain.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational principle of Islamic education: knowledge must be received from living teachers, not merely from texts. The author quotes several early authorities to stress that self-study from books alone leads to error and loss of religion. Understanding this principle helps us appreciate why classical scholars like Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani meticulously selected their teachers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the strong language used to warn against book-only learning.
The passage is short but packed with authoritative quotes.
2
Identify each transmitted quote
The passage contains four distinct sayings from Ibn Jama‘ah, the book al-Shakwa, Mus‘ab ibn al-Zubayr, and Imam al-Shafi‘i. Find each one and note the specific warning it gives.
These quotes form the core evidence for the author's point.
3
Understand why teachers are necessary
Reflect on the reasons given: oral transmission selects the best, books alone distort rulings, and without a teacher one follows Satan. Why is personal guidance so critical in religious sciences?
This connects to the broader theme of isnad (chain of transmission).
4
Connect to Taftazani's practice
The passage ends by stating that Taftazani chose his teachers carefully. How does this conclusion follow from the earlier warnings?
It shows that the principle was not just theory but practiced by great scholars.
5
Apply the lesson personally
Consider your own learning journey: How do you ensure you have qualified guidance? What are the dangers of relying solely on books or internet searches?
This reflection internalizes the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اهتم المسلمون اهتماماً كبيراً بتلقي العلم عن الأساتذة والمدرسين وكرهوا كراهة شديدة أن يتلقى الطالب العلم عن الكتب والقراطيس وحدها، وكان الشيخ الجليل ابن جماعة ( ۱ ) يقول : من أعظم البلية تشيخ الصحيفة. أي أن يتعلم الناس من الصحف والكتب وحدها. وورد في كتاب الشكوى (۲) من لا شيخ له فلا دين له. ومن لم يكن له . أستاذ فإمامه الشيطان، وروي عن مصعب بن الزبير رضي الله عنه أنه كان يقول : إن الناس يتحدثون بأحسن ما يحفظون، ويحفظون أحسن ما يكتبون ويكتبون أحسن ما يسمعون، فإذا أخذت الأدب فخذه من أفواه الرجال، فإنك لا تسمع إلا مختاراً، ولؤلؤاً منثوراً(۳). وروي عن الإمام الشافعي قوله : من تفقه من بطون الكتب، ضيع الأحكام (4) . من أجل ذلك اختار سعد الدين التفتازاني مجموعة من الأساتذة. ومن مشايخ عصره ممن كان يوثق بهم، ولهم بحث وطول اجتماع، وتقتضينا طبيعة البحث أن نلقي ضوءاً على بعض هؤلاء الرجال الأفذاذ.
The Muslims paid great attention to receiving knowledge from masters and teachers, and they intensely disliked that a student should receive knowledge from books and papers alone. The noble Shaykh Ibn Jama‘ah (1) used to say: 'Among the greatest afflictions is the shaykhdom of the page,' meaning that people learn from pages and books alone. It is mentioned in the book 'al-Shakwa' (2): 'He who has no shaykh has no religion; and he who has no teacher, his imam is Satan.' It is narrated from Mus‘ab ibn al-Zubayr, may Allah be pleased with him, that he used to say: 'People speak the best of what they memorize, memorize the best of what they write, and write the best of what they hear. So when you take adab, take it from the mouths of men, for you will hear only the chosen and scattered pearls.' (3) It is narrated from Imam al-Shafi‘i his saying: 'Whoever studies fiqh from the pages of books destroys the rulings.' (4) For this reason Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani chose a group of masters. And among the shaykhs of his time who were trustworthy, who had research and long association, and the nature of our research requires us to shed light on some of these outstanding men.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an introduction explaining why the author will discuss the teachers of Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani. It begins by stating the deep concern of Muslims for acquiring knowledge directly from teachers, and their strong disapproval of relying solely on books. The author then supports this with four transmitted quotes. First, Ibn Jama‘ah says that the worst calamity is for a person to set themselves up as a shaykh based only on reading books. Second, a book titled 'al-Shakwa' states that anyone without a shaykh has no religion, and that the devil is the imam of the one without a teacher. Third, Mus‘ab ibn al-Zubayr advises to take refinement (adab) from the mouths of learned men, because oral transmission selects the best. Fourth, Imam al-Shafi‘i warns that learning fiqh from books alone leads to neglecting proper rulings. The author concludes that because of this, Taftazani carefully selected a group of teachers from his era who were trustworthy and accomplished, and that it is appropriate to highlight some of these exceptional men.

Key Terms
تشيخ الصحيفة
لا شيخ له فلا دين له
الأدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Learning religious sciences entirely from books without a qualified teacher is acceptable and sufficient.
Correction: The passage shows that relying solely on books is considered a great affliction, leads to loss of religion, and destroys correct rulings. A teacher is essential for proper understanding and transmission.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what did Ibn Jama‘ah say about learning from books alone?
A) It is commendable.
B) It is the greatest affliction (تشيخ الصحيفة).
C) It is permissible only for adults.
D) It is better than learning from teachers.

Key Takeaway
Islamic tradition insists that true religious knowledge must be received from qualified teachers, not merely from books, because oral transmission preserves both content and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have a living teacher from whom you are regularly learning the deen? If not, what does that say about the state of your religion according to the warning 'he who has no shaykh has no religion'? Will you seek one this week?
?
Think of a time when you acted on your own understanding of a religious text without consulting a scholar. What was the outcome? Would Imam al-Shafi‘i's warning about 'destroying the rulings' apply to your action?
?
The passage says that the best knowledge is heard from the mouths of men, not read from pages. What is one area of Islam you are currently learning from books or online that you could instead learn directly from a qualified teacher? What will you do about it today?

Editorial Methodology: Five Steps of Textual Criticism
Editor lists five methodological steps used in preparing the critical edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five methodological steps the editor used to prepare a reliable critical edition from multiple manuscripts.

Core Idea
Transparent editing preserves the author's intent.

Before engaging with the text itself, it is important to understand how the editor worked. This passage explains the steps taken to produce a clean, accurate edition while respecting the author's original structure. Knowing this methodology helps you trust the text and understand any editorial interventions.

Guided Path
1
Read the editorial passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the editor lists four main points marked (a)-(d).
The passage is straightforward, but pay attention to the details of each step.
2
Identify the five editorial actions
Even though there are four letters, step (d) includes two separate actions: explaining the book's structure and adding missing titles. Count them as five distinct editorial actions.
3
Understand the role of manuscript variants
Step (b) describes collating three copies and recording variants. This is the core of textual criticism: noting differences to recover the most authentic text.
The editor also mentions 'additions, omissions, and corruptions' in the printed copy.
4
Reflect on editorial transparency
The editor explains why new titles were added (the author often omitted them). This transparency allows readers to know where the editor has intervened.
Consider how this builds trust in the edition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : - ( أ ) قمنا بكتابة النسخة رقم (۱) على حسب قواعد الكتابة الحديثة مع إثبات علامات الترقيم أثناء الكتابة . (ب ) بعد الانتهاء من الكتابة أخذنا في مراجعة النسخة (أ) على النسخة (ب) و (ج) وأثبتنا بالهامش فروق النسخ الموجودة مع الإشارة إلى الزيادة والنقص والتحريفات في النسخة المطبوعة . (ج) حرصنا بقدر الطاقة على تنقية النص من الأخطاء النحوية واللغوية . ( د ) الكتاب قسمه المؤلف إلى مقاصد، والمقاصد إلى فصول، والفصول إلى مباحث. ولكنه أغفل في كثير من الأحيان أن يضع عناوين لكثير من الفصول والمباحث، فقمنا بوضع العناوين الملائمة لها وهناك بعض الموضوعات المتداخلة، والتي رأينا أنها في حاجة إلى عناوين مستقلة، وهذا ما حرصنا عليه .
Secondly: (a) We transcribed copy number (1) according to modern writing rules, with the insertion of punctuation marks during transcription. (b) After completing the transcription, we undertook revision of copy (a) against copies (b) and (c), and we recorded in the margin the variants found, with indication of additions, omissions, and corruptions in the printed copy. (c) We endeavored, to the best of our ability, to purify the text from grammatical and linguistic errors. (d) The author divided the book into maqāṣid (objectives), the maqāṣid into fuṣūl (chapters), and the fuṣūl into mabāḥith (investigations). But he often omitted to place titles for many chapters and investigations, so we placed appropriate titles for them; and there are some overlapping subjects for which we deemed they needed independent titles, and this is what we have done.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage outlines the editor's methodology. It is a list of five procedural steps. (a) They transcribed the base manuscript (copy 1) following modern orthographic conventions and added punctuation. (b) After transcription, they collated the new transcription against the other two manuscripts (b and c) and recorded variants in the margin, noting additions, omissions, and corruptions in the printed edition. (c) They meticulously corrected grammatical and linguistic errors. (d) They explain the original structure: the book is divided into maqāṣid, which are subdivided into fuṣūl, and then into mabāḥith. Since the author often left many sections without headings, the editor provided appropriate titles, especially for overlapping topics that required separate headings. This methodological transparency allows the reader to understand the nature of the editorial intervention.

Key Terms
النسخة رقم (۱)
فروق النسخ
الزيادة والنقص والتحريفات
الأخطاء النحوية واللغوية
المقاصد
الفصول
المباحث
عناوين مستقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the editor changed the author's text arbitrarily or censored it.
Correction: The editor only added titles where the author omitted them and corrected obvious errors; the original wording was preserved and variants were recorded transparently.

Checkpoint
What did the editor do with the titles for chapters and investigations?
A) Removed all titles.
B) Kept them as they were.
C) Added appropriate titles where the author omitted them.
D) Replaced all titles with new ones.

Key Takeaway
The editor's systematic methodology—transcription, collation, correction, and title supplementation—ensures textual accuracy while clarifying the author's intended structure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The editor took great care to note every variant and correction. When you read Islamic texts, do you ever pause to consider the possibility that the words before you may have undergone editorial intervention? How does this awareness affect your trust in what you read, and what responsibility does it place on you to seek out critical editions?
?
The editor added titles to clarify the author's structure, making the book easier to navigate. In your own study of Islamic knowledge, are you relying on others to organize material for you, or do you actively work to understand the structure of what you learn? What can you do today to take greater ownership of your learning?

Four Sources of Sharh al-Maqasid
Enumerating four general sources used in the commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main textual sources that inform the commentary of Sharh al-Maqasid.

Core Idea
A commentator’s toolkit: four works shaping Sharh al-Maqasid.

Every scholarly commentary relies on a foundation of earlier works. This brief passage from Sharh al-Maqasid enumerates four core sources the commentator draws upon. Learning these names gives you a window into the intellectual tradition behind the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the list in Arabic
Read the Arabic passage slowly. Notice the numbered items. They represent distinct works from different fields (theology, philosophy, principles).
The fourth work includes both Muslim and polytheist sects.
2
Identify each work by title
Match each Arabic title to its English translation. Note that 'محصل' means 'summary', 'المطالب العالية' means 'lofty objectives', etc.
3
Understand the scope of each source
The first work covers early and later thinkers (scholars, philosophers, theologians). The second is about higher objectives. The third deals with the ultimate of reason in principles. The fourth surveys the beliefs of various sects.
This shows the breadth of sources: historical, philosophical, theological, and comparative.
4
Reflect on why these sources matter
Ask yourself how each source contributes to a comprehensive theological commentary. The commentator uses these to engage with diverse opinions and arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الدقة : ۱ - محصل أفكار المتقدمين والمتأخرين من العلماء والحكماء والمتكلمين . ۲ - المطالب العالية . ٣ - نهاية المعقول في دراية الأصول. ٤ - اعتقادات فرق المسلمين والمشركين .
Precision: 1- The summary of the ideas of the early and later scholars, philosophers, and theologians. 2- The lofty objectives. 3- The ultimate of reason in the understanding of principles. 4- The beliefs of the sects of Muslims and polytheists.
Source pages p. 147

Commentary
This passage is a list of four works that the commentator of Sharh al-Maqasid has used. The first is 'محصل أفكار المتقدمين والمتأخرين' (The Summary of the Ideas of the Early and Later Thinkers). The second is 'المطالب العالية' (The Lofty Objectives). The third is 'نهاية المعقول في دراية الأصول' (The Ultimate of Reason in the Knowledge of Principles). The fourth is 'اعتقادات فرق المسلمين والمشركين' (The Beliefs of the Sects of Muslims and Polytheists). These works are foundational to the commentary.

المطالب العالية

Key Takeaway
The commentator builds his work on four key sources spanning historical, rational, and comparative theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Textual Variations in Classical Citations
Editor's report on textual fidelity of quotations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the types of textual variations that occur in classical quotations and how editors trace them to original sources.

Core Idea
Not all quotes are verbatim; editors work to restore accuracy.

In classical scholarly works, authors often quote earlier sources, but not always with perfect fidelity. The editor of this text explains the different ways quotes were transmitted and his efforts to verify them. This lesson introduces the common textual issues and the editor's methodology for correction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of textual variation
The passage mentions quotations taken 'by meaning' vs 'verbatim', and those with additions, deletions, or corruptions. Understand each term and its significance.
These variations are common in handwritten transmission.
2
Understand the editor's response
The editor traced each quote to its original source when possible, and noted the book name and page number in the margin. This shows scholarly rigor.
The marginal notes are crucial for verifying the text.
3
Apply the lesson to your own study
Recognize that when you read classical works, you should be aware of possible textual variations. Always check critical editions and marginal notes.
This practice is part of the academic study of Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد وجدنا في كثير من النصوص التي أخذها من هذه الكتب أن بعضها أخذه بالمعنى، والبعض أخذه بالنص، وفي بعضها زيادات أو نقصان أو تحريفات . فعملنا بقدر الطاقة على نقلها من أصولها ، وأشرنا إلى ذلك في الهامش مع إثبات أسماء الكتب وتحديد الصفحات - ما أمكن ذلك - التي يوجد بها النص .
And we found in many of the texts that he took from these books that some he took by meaning, some he took verbatim, and in some there were additions, deletions, or corruptions. So we endeavored within our capacity to transmit them from their originals, and we pointed that out in the margin with the mention of book names and specification of pages—as much as possible—in which the text is found.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the editor's observation on the textual fidelity of the quotations from the sources. The editor notes that many of the texts taken by the commentator from these books were not always literal. Some were taken by meaning (بالمعنى), meaning the commentator paraphrased. Others were taken verbatim (بالنص). In some cases, there were additions, deletions, or corruptions (تحريفات). This is a common issue in classical texts. The editor then describes his methodology: he tried as much as possible to trace these quotes back to their original sources, and he indicated his findings in the margins, including the names of the books and the page numbers where the original text can be found. This shows scholarly diligence in verifying sources.

Key Terms
بالمعنى
بالنص
زيادات
نقصان
تحريفات
الهامش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: تحريفات means intentional distortion or falsification.
Correction: Here تحريفات refers to unintentional textual corruptions in transmission, not deliberate tampering.

Checkpoint
What did the editor do when he found textual variations in the quoted sources?
A) He ignored them
B) He corrected them without indication
C) He traced them to the originals and noted them in the margin
D) He rewrote the entire passage

Key Takeaway
The editor's diligence in tracing quotes to their original sources is a model of scholarly verification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you pass on knowledge or information, do you often convey it exactly as received, or do you find yourself paraphrasing? How might your paraphrasing unintentionally add or delete meaning, and what responsibility does that place on you?
?
The editor traced every quote back to its original source, even when it was taken by meaning. Is there a truth in your life that you have accepted secondhand without verifying? What would it take for you to 'trace it back' to the original source, and are you willing to do so?

Editor's Correction of Corrupted Scholar Names
Editor's note on correcting corrupted names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's methodology in correcting corrupted names and providing biographical references.

Core Idea
Reliable editions require correcting names and providing biographies.

In critical editions of classical texts, one of the editor's key tasks is to restore corrupted names. This short note from the editor of Sharh al-Maqasid explains his approach to identifying and correcting such names, and why he adds biographical information. Understanding this editorial practice helps you appreciate the scholarly rigor behind the text you are studying.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's note
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the editor's claim.
2
Understand 'corrupted names'
The word 'محرفة' means names that have been distorted or incorrectly transmitted. Consider why this happens in manuscripts.
3
Identify the corrective actions
The editor did two things: corrected the names and provided translations (biographies) from primary sources.
4
Appreciate the purpose
The goal is to help the reader identify the scholars and consult further sources if needed.
5
Note the key term 'الترجمة'
Here it means 'biography', not 'translation'. This is a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً. : بعض الأعلام ذكرت محرفة ، فعملنا على تصحيحها والترجمة لها من أمهات المراجع، ليكون الباحث أو القارىء على بينة منها أو الرجوع إلى مصادرها إن أراد الزيادة .
Thirdly: Some proper names were mentioned in a corrupted form, so we worked on correcting them and providing a translation (biography) for them from the mother references, so that the researcher or reader may be aware of them or refer to their sources if he wants more.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the third editorial note. The editor states that some proper names (أعلام) of scholars or figures appeared in the text in a corrupted (محرفة) form. The editor took corrective action: he corrected these names and provided a translation (ترجمة) or biography for each from the primary reference works (أمهات المراجع). The purpose is to enable the researcher or reader to know who these figures are and to allow them to consult the original sources if they wish to learn more. This is a standard scholarly practice in critical editions.

Key Terms
أعلام
محرفة
تصحيحها
الترجمة
أمهات المراجع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'الترجمة' means 'translation' here.
Correction: In this context, 'الترجمة' refers to a biographical notice ('biography'), not a linguistic translation. The editor provides a summary of the scholar's life, not a translation of his name.

Checkpoint
What did the editor do with corrupted names in the text?
A) He left them as they were
B) He deleted them
C) He corrected them and provided biographies
D) He changed them to modern equivalents

Key Takeaway
In critical editions, editors correct corrupted names and provide biographical references from primary sources to ensure clarity and scholarly utility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read Islamic texts, how often do you check the accuracy of the names and references? Are you content with blindly trusting any edition without verifying its editorial standards?
?
The editor corrected corrupted names so that the reader could know the true figures. Have you ever misattributed a quote or a person due to a corrupted name? What steps can you take to ensure your knowledge is accurate?
?
This lesson shows the effort to correct errors in transmission. Reflect on your own learning: are you as meticulous in verifying the sources you learn from as this editor was?

Editorial Work on Hadiths
Editor's description of hadith verification and vowelization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's method of verifying and fully vowelizing hadiths in this classical text.

Core Idea
Hadith verification and vowelization ensure accuracy and clarity.

In this short editorial note, the author outlines two key tasks performed on the hadiths found in the original text. These editorial practices are crucial for producing a reliable and accessible scholarly edition. By examining this passage, we see how meticulous editing preserves the integrity of transmitted knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the concise style of the editor's note.
The passage is a single sentence, so read it carefully.
2
Identify the two editorial actions
The editor mentions two specific actions: takhrij (verification) and tashkil (vowelization). Understand what each entails.
Takhrij involves tracing a hadith to its original sources.
3
Consider the importance of vowelization
Reflect on why adding diacritical marks is necessary for correct recitation and understanding.
Arabic without vowels can be ambiguous.
4
Connect to the broader editorial methodology
Place this note in the context of the editor's overall approach to producing a critical edition.
The editor likely performed similar checks on other elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This is the fourth editorial note. The editor describes his work on the hadiths found in the original text. He performed complete takhrij (تخريج) or verification and sourcing of both the sacred hadiths (الأحاديث القدسية) and the prophetic hadiths (الأحاديث النبوية). Additionally, he added full diacritical marks (ضبطنا كلماتها بالشكل) to the words of the hadiths, which is important for correct pronunciation and to avoid ambiguity. This editorial effort enhances the usability and scholarly value of the edition.

Editor enumerates four specific books of Ibn Sina used by the author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which specific works of Ibn Sina the author of Sharh al-Maqasid relied upon and why these sources are significant.

Core Idea
The author heavily used Ibn Sina's philosophical and medical works.

In this passage, the editor of Sharh al-Maqasid lists the exact books of Ibn Sina that the author consulted. This is important because it shows the influence of Avicennan philosophy on Islamic theology. The list includes major works like al-Shifa and al-Isharat. Understanding this helps us see the intellectual sources behind the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

2
Identify the four books
The passage presents four books. Find each one and note their full titles and any specific sections mentioned (e.g., al-Shifa's section on dialectic and logic).
Keep in mind that these are works of Ibn Sina, a major philosopher and physician.
3
Consider the topics where these books were used
The editor notes that the author used these books especially when writing the fourth and fifth aims (maqasid). The topics include inner senses, abstract entities, celestial and human souls, etc. Reflect on why these philosophical works would be relevant to those discussions.
This shows the integration of peripatetic philosophy into Islamic theological discourse.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage lists the specific books of Ibn Sina that the author of Sharh al-Maqasid relied upon. Correcting Corrupted Names in Manuscripts
Editor explains method for correcting distorted names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's method for correcting corrupted names and providing biographical context to ensure accurate identification of historical figures.

Core Idea
Historical accuracy requires correcting names and identifying figures from authoritative sources.

Editors of classical texts often encounter corrupted names in manuscripts. This lesson explores a methodological note where the editor explains his approach: correcting the names and providing biographical information from major reference works. This ensures readers can properly identify the individuals and consult further sources if needed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

The passage is brief but packed with methodological intent.
2
Understand the editor's motivation
The editor states that some notable names were mentioned in a distorted form ('ذكرت محرفة'). Ask yourself: why would a manuscript have corrupted names?
Common reasons: scribal errors, dialect variations, or faded ink.
3
Identify the corrective actions
The editor takes two actions: correcting the names ('تصحيحها') and providing biographical information ('الترجمة لها') from major reference works ('أمهات المراجع').
This dual approach restores accuracy and aids research.
4
Reflect on the pedagogical purpose
The editor's goal is to enable the researcher or reader to be informed ('على بينة') and to refer to original sources if they want more ('الرجوع إلى مصادرها').
This reflects a commitment to scholarly transparency.

Editor's Method for Tracing Sects and Poetry
Editor outlines two final editorial tasks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's dual approach to identifying sources for Islamic sects and poems in the commentary.

Core Idea
The editor's notes trace the author's sources for sects and poetry.

This passage reveals the final steps of the editor's methodology: locating the original statements of Islamic sects and attributing the poems cited by the author. These tasks demonstrate the editor's commitment to providing a critical apparatus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the two numbered tasks. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the two tasks
Notice that the editor indicates two separate editorial actions: one concerning sectarian statements (النقول) and one concerning poetic lines (الأشعار).
These are numbered 'Eighthly' and 'Ninthly'.
3
Understand the rationale
Consider why locating the original sources enhances the scholarly value of the edition. It allows readers to verify claims and see the author's engagement with primary texts.
4
Reflect on common misunderstanding
Many readers might think the editor only worked on sects. This passage shows poetry was also handled. Check the misconception question to clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثامناً : ناقش المؤلف الفرق الإسلامية، معتمداً على بعض نقولهم فأشرنا بقدر الطاقة إلى أماكن هذه النقول . تاسعاً : نسبنا الأشعار التي استشهد بها المؤلف إلى قائلها وأرشدنا إلى بعض المراجع التي توجد بها .

Commentary
Two final editorial tasks are described. First, for the author's discussions of Islamic sects, the editor attempted to locate the original sources of the sectarian positions mentioned. Second, for the poetic quotations scattered throughout the work, the editor identified the poets and provided references to where the poems can be found. This enhances the scholarly value of the edition by tracing the author's sources.

The Five Chapters and Six Objectives of Ilm al-Kalam
Enumeration of chapters and explanation of maqasid arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the discipline of kalam is structured into five main chapters and six objectives, and why this organization is logically necessary.

Core Idea
The chapters of kalam mirror the hierarchy of existence: general, substance, accident, divine, and revealed.

The author begins by defining the scope of kalam: knowledge of the Creator, His attributes, actions, prophethood, and the afterlife, plus matters beyond independent reason. From this domain, he derives five essential chapters. Understanding this classification is key to grasping the entire theological project, as it determines how topics are arranged and why some discussions appear in specific sections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of kalam
Read the opening sentence to see what kalam covers: knowledge of Allah, His attributes, actions, prophethood, and the afterlife, as well as matters inaccessible to independent reason and the states of substances and accidents.
This scope justifies the chapters that follow.
2
Memorize the five chapters
Locate the list: 1) General Matters, 2) Accidents, 3) Substances, 4) Divine Matters, 5) Revealed Matters. Understand that 'General Matters' is a preface covering overarching concepts.
These five are the backbone of classical kalam manuals.
3
Explore the six maqasid
The author then shows how these chapters map to six objectives: first (principles), second (general matters), third (accidents), fourth (substances), fifth (divine), sixth (revealed). Notice the logic: topics not specific to one category come second, specific ones in order of ontological precedence.
Maqasid 1 covers introductory material not part of the five core chapters.
4
Understand the rationale for ordering
The author explains that later topics depend on earlier ones in some proofs, and that sometimes a topic from a later chapter is placed earlier for coherence (e.g., vision in divine matters).
This reveals the pedagogical concern behind the arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاقتصر المليون (۱) على ما يتعلق بمعرفة الصانع وصفاته وأفعاله وما يتفرع على ذلك من النبوة والمعاد. وسائر مالا سبيل (۲) للعقل باستقلاله ، وما يترتب عليه إثبات ذلك من الأحوال المختصة بالجواهر والأعراض أو الشاملة لأكثر الموجودات فجاءت أبواب الكلام خمسة هي : الأمور العامة والأعراض والجواهر، والإلهيات والسمعيات . وقد جرت العادة بتصديرها بمباحث تجري مجرى السوابق لها تسمى بالمبادىء فرتبنا الكتاب على ستة مقاصد ، ووجه الضبط أن المذكور (۳) فيه إن كان من مقاصد الكلام فإما سمعيات وهو المقصد السادس، أو عقليات مختص بالواجب وهو الخامس، أو بالممكن الجوهر وهو الرابع ، أو العرض وهو الثالث، أو لا مختص (٤) بواحد وهو الثاني ، وإن لم يكن من مقاصد الفن فهو المقصد الأول من الكتاب، ووجه الترتيب توقف اللاحق على السابق، في بعض البينات (٥) ، وقد يقتضي الضبط والمناسبة إيراد شيء من مباحث تأتي (٦) في الآخر كمسألة الرؤية في الإلهيات، وإعادة المعدوم في السمعيات . (۱) في (ب) الملبون وهو تحريف . (۲) زيد في (ب) كلمة (إليه). (۳) سقطت من (ب) كلمة (أن) . (٤) في (ب) أو لا يختص . (٥) في (ب) البيانات . (٦) في (ب) باب في آخر .
The Muslims (al-milliyyun) limited themselves to what pertains to knowledge of the Maker, His attributes, and His actions, and what branches from that: prophethood and the afterlife. And everything that the intellect has no independent way to [know], and what entails proof of that from the states specific to substances and accidents or encompassing most existents. Thus the chapters of kalam (abwab al-kalam) became five: General Matters (al-umur al-amma), Accidents (al-a'rad), Substances (al-jawahir), Divine Matters (al-ilahiyyat), and Revealed Matters (al-sam'iyyat). And the custom has been to precede them with investigations that serve as preliminaries called 'the principles' (al-mabadi'). So we arranged the book into six objectives (maqasid). And the rationale for this organization is that whatever is mentioned in it, if it is from the objectives of kalam, then either it is revealed matters (sam'iyyat), which is the sixth objective; or rational matters specific to the Necessary Being (al-wajib), which is the fifth; or to the possible substance (al-mumkin al-jawhar), which is the fourth; or to the accident (al-'arad), which is the third; or not specific to any one, which is the second. And if it is not from the objectives of the art, then it is the first objective of the book. And the rationale for the order is the dependence of the later on the earlier in some proofs. And sometimes organization and suitability require including some topics that come later in the earlier sections, like the issue of vision (ru'ya) in Divine Matters, and the re-creation of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum) in Revealed Matters. (1) In copy (ب): 'al-mullabun' and it is a distortion. (2) In copy (ب): added the word (إليه). (3) In copy (ب): the word (أن) is missing. (4) In copy (ب): 'or not specific' (or 'or does not specialize'). (5) In copy (ب): 'al-bayanat' (the proofs? instead of 'al-binat'). (6) In copy (ب): 'a chapter in the last'.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines the scope and structure of kalam as a discipline. The author begins by noting that Muslim scholars (al-milliyyun) focused their efforts on knowledge of the Creator, His attributes, and His actions, along with derivative topics like prophethood and the afterlife. The domain of kalam also includes matters that the intellect cannot independently grasp and the evidentiary states pertaining to substances and accidents. From this, the author enumerates the five chapters (abwab) of kalam: (1) General Matters (al-umum al-amma), (2) Accidents (al-a'rad), (3) Substances (al-jawahir), (4) Divine Matters (al-ilahiyyat), and (5) Revealed Matters (al-sam'iyyat). These five core chapters are typically preceded by introductory investigations called al-mabadi' (the principles). The author then explains how he arranged his own book, Sharh al-Maqasid, into six objectives (maqasid), corresponding to these divisions. The first maqasid covers introductory material not part of kalam's core objectives. The second maqasid covers general matters that are not specific to any one category. The third covers accidents, the fourth covers possible substances, the fifth covers the Necessary Being, and the sixth covers revealed matters. This ordering is logical because later topics depend on earlier ones in some proofs. The author also notes that sometimes it is necessary to include a topic from a later section in an earlier one for coherence, such as the discussion of the beatific vision (ru'ya) in the divine section or the restoration of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum) in the revealed section. The passage ends with textual notes that record variant readings from different manuscripts, indicating the author's scholarly care in establishing the text.

Correction: The passage shows that the structure derives from the subject matter itself: general, specific to substances/accidents, divine, and revealed. Moreover, ordering follows logical dependence and pedagogical suitability, making it a principled classification.

The Six Maqasid of Sharh al-Maqasid: Structure and Rationale
Taftazani explains the arrangement of his book's six maqasid and the rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani organizes his book into six maqasid, and the reasoning behind the order of these sections.

Core Idea
The six maqasid are arranged by logical dependence: preliminaries, then general, then specific, then revelational.

Taftazani based the structure of Sharh al-Maqasid on the traditional five chapters of kalam, but reorganized them into six maqasid with a clear rationale. In this passage, he explains how each maqsad relates to the subject matter of kalam and why they appear in that order. Understanding this framework helps you navigate the entire book.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional five chapters
Read the passage and note the five chapters that traditionally constitute kalam: universal matters, accidents, substances, divine things, and revelational matters.
Taftazani mentions they are prefaced by 'principles' (mabadi).
2
Map the six maqasid to the chapters
The six maqasid are: 1) preliminaries (not part of kalam proper), 2) universal matters, 3) accidents, 4) substances, 5) divine things, 6) revelational matters. See how the first is an introduction, and the rest correspond to the five chapters.
The second maqsad covers what is not specific to any one category.
3
Understand the ordering rationale
The order is based on dependence: later maqasid depend on earlier ones in proofs. Also note that sometimes later topics are mentioned earlier for appropriateness, e.g., vision of God in divine things.
This shows Taftazani's methodological flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاقتصر المليون على ما يتعلق بمعرفة الصانع وصفاته وأفعاله وما يتفرع على ذلك من النبوة والمعاد. وسائر ما لا سبيل للعقل باستقلاله، وما يترتب عليه إثبات ذلك من الأحوال المختصة بالجواهر والأعراض أو الشاملة لأكثر الموجودات فجاءت أبواب الكلام خمسة هي: الأمور العامة والأعراض والجواهر، والإلهيات والسمعيات. وقد جرت العادة بتصديرها بمباحث تجري مجرى السوابق لها تسمى بالمبادئ فرتبنا الكتاب على ستة مقاصد، ووجه الضبط أن المذكور فيه إن كان من مقاصد الكلام فإما سمعيات وهو المقصد السادس، أو عقليات مختص بالواجب وهو الخامس، أو بالممكن الجوهر وهو الرابع، أو العرض وهو الثالث، أو لا مختص بواحد وهو الثاني، وإن لم يكن من مقاصد الفن فهو المقصد الأول من الكتاب، ووجه الترتيب توقف اللاحق على السابق، في بعض البينات، وقد يقتضي الضبط والمناسبة إيراد شيء من مباحث تأتي في الآخر كمسألة الرؤية في الإلهيات، وإعادة المعدوم في السمعيات.
The [؟] confined themselves to what relates to knowledge of the Maker, His attributes, His actions, and what branches from that of prophethood and the afterlife. And the rest of what the intellect has no independent way to [know], and what depends on establishing that from the states specific to substances and accidents or common to most existents, so the chapters of kalam became five: universal matters, accidents, substances, divine things, and revelational matters. And custom has been to preface them with discussions that serve as preliminaries for them, called the principles. So we arranged the book into six maqasid. And the manner of organization is that what is mentioned therein, if it is from the maqasid of kalam, then either it is revelational matters, which is the sixth maqsad, or rational matters: specific to the Necessary, which is the fifth; or to the possible: the substance, which is the fourth; or the accident, which is the third; or not specific to one, which is the second. And if it is not from the maqasid of the art, then it is the first maqsad of the book. And the manner of ordering is the dependence of the later on the earlier in some of the proofs. And organization and appropriateness may require bringing forward something from discussions that come later, such as the issue of vision in divine things, and the restoration of the non-existent in revelational matters.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation of how the author, Taftazani, structured his book Sharh al-Maqasid. It begins by noting that earlier scholars ( perhaps 'the later ones' but the text has 'المليون' which is unclear) confined themselves to topics concerning knowledge of the Creator, His attributes, and His actions, along with what branches from that, namely prophethood and the afterlife, and other matters beyond the independent reach of reason. From these topics, the traditional five chapters of kalam emerged: universal matters (al-umur al-'amma), accidents (al-a'rad), substances (al-jawahir), divine things (al-ilahiyat), and revelational matters (al-sam'iyyat). Customarily, these chapters are preceded by introductory discussions called the principles (al-mabadi'). The author then states he arranged his book into six maqasid (goals or sections). He explains the logical organization: anything that belongs to the maqasid of kalam is either sam'iyyat (the sixth maqsad) or rational. The rational is further divided by what it is specific to: if specific to the Necessary (al-wajib), it is the fifth maqsad; if specific to a possible substance, it is the fourth; if specific to a possible accident, it is the third; if not specific to any one of these, it is the second. Anything that does not belong to the subject matter of kalam at all is placed in the first maqsad, which serves as an introduction. The order of these maqasid is based on the dependence of later material on earlier material in some proofs. However, decorum and appropriateness may sometimes require presenting a topic that is later, such as discussing the vision of God (al-ru'ya) in the chapter on divine things, or the restoration of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum) in the chapter on revelational matters, even though these are logically later.

أمور عامة
أعراض
جواهر
إلهيات
سمعيات
مبادئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All six maqasid are equally part of kalam's subject matter.
Correction: The first maqsad (preliminaries) is not part of kalam proper but an introduction; only the second through sixth belong to kalam's subject matter.

Checkpoint

B) Six maqasid, with revelational matters as the sixth

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's six maqasid are logically ordered so that later sections build on earlier ones, with the first serving as an introduction to the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Structure of the First Maqsad: Principles and Chapters
Sanusi explains the division of the first maqsad into three chapters and the rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first maqsad of the book is structured into three chapters and why this division reflects the two types of principles: general preliminaries and kalam-specific topics.

Core Idea
The first maqsad splits into three chapters for two kinds of principles: universal preliminaries and kalam-specific investigations.

This lesson opens the first major aim (maqsad) of the book, which deals with the foundational principles of kalam. The author, al-Sanusi, explains that these principles fall into two categories: those that preface every science (definition, subject, goal) and those that are particular to the science of kalam (knowledge and reasoning). He devotes one chapter to the former and two chapters to the latter, totalling three chapters. Understanding this structure helps us see how kalam builds its method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's explanation of the three-chapter division.
2
Identify the two types of principles
Notice that the author distinguishes between general preliminaries (definition, subject, goal) common to all sciences and kalam-specific topics (knowledge and reasoning).
3
Understand the rationale
The author explains that kalam-specific topics are needed because acquiring beliefs through reasoning and refuting deniers depends on them, and no other Islamic science is more fitting to explain them.
4
Reflect on the structure
Consider why the author placed the general preliminaries in one chapter and the kalam-specific topics in two chapters, making three total.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : في المبادىء. وفيه فصول ثلاثة(۱). الأول في المقدمات (۲)) . الفصل الأول في المقدمات أقول : رتبته (۳) على ثلاثة فصول لأن المبادىء منها ما رأوا تصدير كل (٤) علم بها كمعرفة حده وموضوعه وغايته (٥) ونحو ذلك فسماها ، بالمقدمات، وجعلها في فصل ومنها ما صدروا بها علم الكلام خاصة كمباحث العلم والنظر، لأن تحصيل العقائد بطريق النظر والاستدلال والرد على منكري حصول العلم أصلا، واستفادته من النظر مطلقاً ، أو في الإلهيات خاصة يتوقف على ذلك، وليس في العلوم الإسلامية ما هو أليق ببيانه فجعلها في فصلين.
The first aim: concerning the principles. It contains three chapters. The first is on the preliminaries. The first chapter: on the preliminaries. I say: He arranged it into three chapters because among the principles are those that they saw to preface every science with, such as knowing its definition, subject, and goal and the like, so they called them 'preliminaries' and placed them in one chapter. And among them are those with which they prefaced specifically the science of kalam, such as the investigations of knowledge and reasoning, because the acquisition of beliefs by way of reasoning and inference and the refutation of those who deny the attainment of knowledge at all, and its derivation from reasoning absolutely, or specifically in divine matters, depends on that. And there is no Islamic science more fitting to explain it, so he placed them in two chapters.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of the first maqsad (aim) of the book, which is about the principles (al-mabadi'). The author states that the first maqsad contains three chapters. He then explains that principles are of two types: general principles that preface every science, such as its definition (hadd), subject (mawdu'), and goal (ghaya), which are called 'preliminaries' (al-muqaddimat) and are placed in the first chapter. The second type consists of principles specific to the science of kalam, namely the investigations of knowledge (al-ilm) and reasoning (al-nazar), because the acquisition of beliefs through reasoning and the refutation of those who deny knowledge or its derivation from reasoning depend on these investigations. Since no other Islamic science is more appropriate for explaining these topics, the author devotes two chapters to them, making a total of three chapters. The logic is that the general preliminaries are common to all sciences, while the specific ones are needed for kalam's method.

Key Terms
المقصد
المبادىء
المقدمات
حده
موضوعه
غايته
العلم
النظر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preliminaries only refer to the definition of a science.
Correction: Preliminaries include the definition, subject, and goal of a science, as well as other introductory matters.

Checkpoint
Why are the investigations of knowledge and reasoning placed in separate chapters in the first maqsad?
A) They are general to all sciences.
B) They are specific to kalam and needed for its method.
C) They are unrelated to kalam.
D) They are part of logic only.

Key Takeaway
The first maqsad of the book is structured into three chapters: one for general preliminaries and two for kalam-specific investigations of knowledge and reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi separates universal preliminaries from kalam-specific principles. Have you ever taken time to identify the 'definition, subject, and goal' of your own faith? If not, what might that reveal about how seriously you engage with your beliefs? Will you commit to writing down your understanding of these three today?
?
The author stresses that acquiring sound beliefs depends on proper reasoning (nazar). Where in your life have you accepted beliefs without careful thought or evidence? What is one area of your creed you have not examined using reasoning? Decide now to study it using the method taught in this book.
?
This passage claims that no Islamic science is more fitting to explain knowledge and reasoning than kalam. Do you treat theology as the foundation of your intellectual life, or as a secondary interest? If it is not central, what change will you make starting this week to give it the priority it deserves?

The Arrangement of the First Objective's Three Chapters
The author explains why he divided the first objective into three chapters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author divided the first objective into three chapters based on the nature of preliminaries.

Core Idea
The three chapters mirror two types of preliminaries: general and kalām-specific.

This lesson explores the author's rationale for structuring the opening chapters of his work. Before diving into the specific doctrines of kalām, he explains that certain foundational concepts must be addressed first. Some of these are common to all sciences, while others are unique to kalām. Let's see how he organizes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very logical structure.
2
Identify the two types of preliminaries
The passage distinguishes between general introductory matters (like definition, subject, purpose) and kalām-specific topics (knowledge and reasoning). Find where each is mentioned.
The general ones are called 'muhimmat' and the specific ones are 'mabāhis al-ilm wa al-nazar'.
3
Understand why two chapters for kalām-specific topics
The author argues that the acquisition of beliefs by reasoning, and refutation of deniers, depends on these investigations. Since no other Islamic science is more fitting, he dedicates two separate chapters.
This shows the central role of epistemology in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول: في المبادىء. وفيه فصول ثلاثة. الأول في المقدمات. الفصل الأول في المقدمات. أقول: رتبته على ثلاثة فصول لأن المبادىء منها ما رأوا تصدير كل علم بها كمعرفة حده وموضوعه وغايته ونحو ذلك فسماها بالمقدمات، وجعلها في فصل ومنها ما صدروا بها علم الكلام خاصة كمباحث العلم والنظر، لأن تحصيل العقائد بطريق النظر والاستدلال والرد على منكري حصول العلم أصلا، واستفادته من النظر مطلقاً، أو في الإلهيات خاصة يتوقف على ذلك، وليس في العلوم الإسلامية ما هو أليق ببيانه فجعلها في فصلين.
The first objective: concerning the preliminaries. And it contains three chapters. The first is on the introductory matters. The first chapter: on the introductory matters. I say: I arranged it in three chapters because among the preliminaries are those which they saw as making every science begin with them, such as knowing its definition, subject matter, and purpose and the like, so they called them 'introductory matters' and placed them in one chapter; and among them are those with which they began the science of kalām specifically, such as the investigations of knowledge and reasoning, because the acquisition of beliefs through the method of reasoning and inference, and the refutation of those who deny the attainment of knowledge at all, and its benefit from reasoning absolutely, or in the divine matters specifically, depends on that, and there is no Islamic science more fitting for its exposition, so he placed them in two chapters.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the author's rationale for structuring the first objective (al-maqṣad al-awwal) of his work into three chapters. He explains that the preliminaries (mabādiʾ) are of two types. The first type consists of those preliminary matters that every science begins with, such as the definition, subject matter, and purpose of the science. These are called 'introductory matters' (muqaddimāt) and are placed in one chapter. The second type consists of those preliminaries that are specific to the science of kalām, namely the investigations of knowledge (al-ʿilm) and reasoning (al-naẓar). The author notes that acquiring beliefs through reasoning and inference, as well as refuting those who deny the possibility of knowledge or its derivation from reasoning (whether absolutely or specifically in divine matters), depends on these investigations. Since no other Islamic science is more appropriate for explaining these topics, he dedicates two separate chapters to them—one on knowledge and one on reasoning. Thus the three chapters cover: general introductory matters, knowledge, and reasoning.

Key Terms
مبادىء
مقدمات
تصدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division into three chapters is arbitrary or based on convenience.
Correction: The author explains it is based on a logical distinction between general preliminaries common to all sciences and specific preliminaries unique to kalām.

Checkpoint
Why did the author place the investigations of knowledge and reasoning in two separate chapters rather than in the single chapter on introductory matters?
A) Because they are not considered preliminaries.
B) Because they are specific to kalām and require detailed exposition.
C) Because the author forgot to include them in the first chapter.
D) Because they are not useful for acquiring beliefs.

Key Takeaway
The first objective's three chapters are structured according to two categories of preliminaries: general introductory matters (chapter 1) and kalām-specific topics of knowledge and reasoning (chapters 2 and 3).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author argues that knowledge and reasoning are necessary for acquiring correct beliefs. Have you ever tried to convince someone of a theological truth without first clarifying what knowledge means or how reasoning works? What obstacle did you face?
?
If you were to write a book on Islamic beliefs, would you follow a similar structure of setting up preliminaries? What would you include, and why does that matter for your own learning journey?

The Subject Defines and Unifies a Science
Al-Taftazani argues for the subject as the basis of scientific distinction and unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that the subject (mawdu') is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues.

Core Idea
The subject is the heart of every science—it gives identity and holds the issues together.

In this opening part of his discussion, al-Taftazani states the consensus that sciences are differentiated by their subjects. He then explains why it is appropriate to begin a science by stating its subject: it provides both conceptual and essential distinction, and it reveals the aspect of unity among the diverse issues of that science, guiding the student to what belongs and what does not. This principle establishes the subject as the primary unifying factor for the issues of a science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the key claim that the subject is the aspect of unity for issues.
Notice the logical flow from consensus to verification.
2
Identify the main argument
Find where al-Taftazani states that the subject is the aspect of unity for issues of a science. Understand why he emphasizes this.
He says: 'There is no doubt that the aspect of unity of the issues of a science primarily and essentially is the subject.'
3
Understand the process of scientific inquiry
He describes how scholars study realities, obtain propositions (issues), and group them under subjects. See how this leads to the formation of distinct sciences.
The subject can be the reality itself, a part, a type, or an essential accident.
4
Reflect on secondary aspects
He acknowledges that definitions and purposes also provide unity but emphasizes that the subject is primary in essence.
The subject is the basis for differentiation in themselves, even if definitions serve for the student.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : اتفقت كلمة القوم على أن التمييز العلوم في أنفسها إنما هو بحسب تمايز الموضوعات فناسب تصدير العلم ببيان الموضوع لإفادة لما به يتميز بحسب الذات بعد ما أفاد التعريف التمييز بحسب المفهوم، وأيضاً في معرفة جهة الوحدة للكثرة المطلوبة إحاطة بها إجمالاً بحيث إذا قصد تحصيل تفاصيلها لم ينصرف الطلب عما هو منها إلى ما ليس منها، ولا شك أن جهة وحدة مسائل العلم أولاً وبالذات هو الموضوع إذ فيه اشتراكها وبه اتحادها على ما سنفصله، وتحقيق المقام أنهم لما حاولوا معرفة أحوال الأشياء بقدر الطاقة البشرية على ما هو المراد بالحكمة وصنعوا الحقائق أنواعاً وأجناساً وغيرها، كالإنسان والحيوان والموجود وبحثوا عن أحوالها المختصة وأثبتوها لها بالأدلة فحصلت لهم قضايا كسبية محمولاتها أعراض ذاتية لتلك الحقائق، سموها بالمسائل، وجعلوا كل طائفة منها يرجع إلى واحد من تلك الأشياء بأن تكون موضوعاتها نفسه أو جزءاً له أو نوعاً منه أو عرضاً ذاتياً له علماً خاصاً يفرد بالتدوين والتسمية والتعليم نظراً إلى ما لتلك الطائفة على كثرتها، واختلاف محمولاتها من الاتحاد من جهة الموضوع أي الاشتراك فيه على الوجه المذكور ثم قد يتحد من جهات أخرى كالمنفعة والغاية ونحوهما، ويؤخذ لها من بعض تلك الجهات ما يفيد تصورها إجمالاً، ومن حيث إن لها وحدة فيكون حداً للعلم إن دل على حقيقة مسماه أعني ذلك المركب الاعتباري كما يقال: هو علم يبحث فيه عن كذا أو علم بقواعد كذا، وإلا فرسماً كما يقال هو علم يقتدر به على كذا أو يحترز عن كذا، أو يكون آلة لكذا، فظهر أن الموضوع هو جهة وحدة مسائل العلم الواحد نظراً إلى ذاتها وإن عرضت لها جهات أخر كالتعريف والغاية، فإنه لا معنى لكون هذا علماً وذاك علماً آخر سوى أنه يبحث هذا عن أحوال شيء وذلك عن أحوال شيء آخر مغاير له بالذات أو بالاعتبار، فلا يكون تمايز العلوم في أنفسها، وبالنظر إلى ذواتها إلا بحسب الموضوع، وإن كانت تتمايز عند الطالب بما لها من التعريفات والغايات ونحوهما، ولهذا جعلوا تباين العلوم وتناسبها وتداخلها أيضاً بحسب الموضوع، بمعنى أن موضوع أحد العلمين إن كان مبايناً لموضوع الآخر من كل وجه فالعلمان متباينان على الإطلاق، وإن كان أعم منه، فالعلمان متداخلان وإن كان موضوعهما شيئاً واحداً بالذات متغايراً بالاعتبار أو شيئين متشاركين في جنس أو غيره، فالعلمان متناسبان على تفاصيل ذكرت في موضعها وبالجملة فقد أطبقوا على امتناع أن يكون شيء واحد موضوعاً لعلمين من غير اعتبار تغاير، بأن يؤخذ في أحدهما مطلقاً، وفي الآخر مقيداً أو يؤخذ في كل منهما مقيداً بقيد آخر، وامتناع أن يكون موضوع علم واحد شيئين من غير اعتبار اتحادهما في جنس أو غاية أو غيرهما، إذ لا معنى لاتحاد العلم واختلافه بدون.
I say: The consensus of the scholars is that the differentiation of the sciences in themselves is only according to the differentiation of their subjects. So it is appropriate to begin a science by stating its subject to benefit what distinguishes it in terms of essence, after the definition gave the distinction in terms of concept. Also, to know the aspect of unity for the multiplicity that one aims to encompass in summary, so that when one intends to obtain its details, the quest does not turn from what belongs to it to what does not. There is no doubt that the aspect of unity of the issues of a science primarily and essentially is the subject, for in it is their commonality and by it is their unity, as we will detail. The verification of the matter is that when they attempted to know the states of things as much as human capacity allows, which is intended by wisdom, and they made the realities into types and genera and others, like human, animal, and existent, and they researched their specific states and proved them for them with evidence, so there resulted for them acquired propositions whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities; they called them 'issues' (masa'il). And they made each group of them that goes back to one of those things—whether its subject is itself, a part of it, a type of it, or an essential accident of it—a particular science, distinguished by compilation, naming, and teaching, considering what that group, despite its multiplicity and the difference of its predicates, has of unity from the aspect of the subject, i.e., commonality in it in the mentioned manner. Then it may be unified from other aspects, like benefit, purpose, and the like. And for it there is taken from some of those aspects what benefits its conception in summary, and insofar as it has unity, it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named object, i.e., that composite conceptual thing, as is said: 'It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated' or 'a science of such-and-such rules'; otherwise it is a description (rasm), as is said: 'It is a science by which one is empowered to do such-and-such' or 'by which one guards against such-and-such', or 'it is a tool for such-and-such'. Thus it became clear that the subject is the aspect of unity of the issues of a single science in terms of their essence, even if they have other aspects like definition and purpose. For there is no meaning to one thing being a science and another being a different science except that this one investigates the states of one thing and that one investigates the states of another thing different from it essentially or by consideration. So the differentiation of the sciences in themselves and with respect to their essences is only according to the subject, even if they are differentiated for the student through their definitions, purposes, and the like. For this reason they made the divergence (tabayun), proportionality (tanasub), and overlap (tadakhul) of sciences also according to the subject, meaning that if the subject of one of two sciences is completely divergent from the subject of the other, then the two sciences are absolutely divergent; if it is more general than it, then they are overlapping; and if their subject is one thing essentially but differs by consideration, or two things sharing in a genus or otherwise, then the two sciences are proportional, with details mentioned in their place. In summary, they have agreed on the impossibility of one thing being the subject of two sciences without consideration of difference, such as taking it absolute in one and restricted in the other, or taking it in each restricted by another restriction; and the impossibility of the subject of one science being two things without consideration of their unity in genus, purpose, or otherwise, for there is no meaning to the unity and difference of a science without that.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument by al-Taftazani explaining why the subject (mawdu') is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues. He begins by stating the consensus that sciences are differentiated by their subjects, so it is fitting to begin a science by stating its subject. The subject provides both conceptual and essential distinction, and also reveals the aspect of unity among the diverse issues of that science, preventing the student from straying into unrelated matters. He then verifies the matter by describing the process of scientific inquiry: scholars study the states of things (realities) and obtain propositions (issues) whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities. They then group these issues under a single subject (whether the subject itself, its part, its type, or its essential accident) and designate that group as a particular science. The unity of these issues under the subject is primary, though other unities (like benefit or purpose) may also be considered. The subject may be used to define the science (hadd) if it indicates the reality of the science, or to describe it (rasm) if it indicates the power or purpose. He concludes that the differentiation of sciences in themselves is only by the subject, even though students may differentiate them by definitions or purposes. This leads to the classification of sciences as divergent, overlapping, or proportional based on the relationship between their subjects. Finally, he notes the consensus that a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some consideration of difference, and a single science cannot have two subjects without some unifying consideration.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu')
مسائل (masa'il)
تمييز (tamyiz)
جهة وحدة (jiha al-wahda)
حد (hadd)
رسم (rasm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sciences are distinguished merely by their definitions or purposes.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that the true distinction is by the subject (mawdu') in essence, while definitions and purposes are secondary and serve for the student's understanding.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the primary factor that distinguishes one science from another?
A) The definition of the science
B) The purpose (ghaya) of the science
C) The subject (mawdu') of the science
D) The benefit of the science

Key Takeaway
The subject is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues, even though definitions and purposes may also serve as secondary aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new science, do you first ask 'What is its subject?' or do you just dive into its issues? How would starting with the subject change your understanding? Make a commitment to always ask 'What is the subject?' before studying any discipline, starting with your next lesson.
?
Your knowledge of deen—what is its subject? Is it merely about rules or about knowing Allah? Pause and write down: what single subject unifies everything you learn in your faith? If you can't name it, you may be collecting scattered facts without unity. Resolve to identify the subject of your religious learning today.
?
Have you ever confused the purpose of a science with its true subject? For example, treating kalam as merely a tool to debate rather than a science about the states of existence? Search your heart: have you studied kalam for intellectual pride or for knowing the truth? Repent and reorient your intention to seek the subject itself—the reality of things as they are.

How Subjects Determine Scientific Relationships
Al-Taftazani classifies scientific relationships based on subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani classifies the relationships between sciences (divergent, overlapping, proportional) based on their subjects, and the rules regarding one subject for two sciences and two subjects for one science.

Core Idea
The subject is the basis for classifying sciences as divergent, overlapping, or proportional.

After establishing that the subject is the primary factor for distinguishing sciences, al-Taftazani now applies this principle to explain the possible relationships between any two sciences. He introduces three categories: tabayun (divergence), tadakhul (overlap), and tanasub (proportionality), all defined by the relationship between their subjects. He also states two important impossibilities: one thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some distinction, and a single science cannot have two subjects without a unifying factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relationships
Read the second part starting from 'ولهذا جعلوا تباين العلوم...' and its translation. Focus on the three categories.
Notice the three terms: tabayun, tadakhul, tanasub.
2
Identify the three categories
For each category (divergent, overlapping, proportional), find the condition given in terms of subject relationships.
Divergent: subjects are completely different; Overlapping: one subject is more general; Proportional: subjects share a genus or are one thing under different considerations.
3
Understand the two impossibilities
He states two impossibilities: one thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some difference in consideration, and one science cannot have two subjects without a unifying factor.
These reinforce that the subject is the defining element.
4
Reflect on the overall principle
Consider how this classification shows that the subject is central to scientific identity and relationships.
The entire classification is built on the relationship between subjects.

من جهات أخرى كالمنفعة والغاية ونحوهما، ويؤخذ لها من بعض تلك الجهات ما يفيد تصورها إجمالاً، ومن حيث إن لها وحدة فيكون حداً للعلم إن دل على حقيقة مسماه أعني ذلك المركب الاعتباري كما يقال: هو علم يبحث فيه عن كذا أو علم بقواعد كذا، وإلا فرسماً كما يقال هو علم يقتدر به على كذا أو يحترز عن كذا، أو يكون آلة لكذا، فظهر أن الموضوع هو جهة وحدة مسائل العلم الواحد نظراً إلى ذاتها وإن عرضت لها جهات أخر كالتعريف والغاية، فإنه لا معنى لكون هذا علماً وذاك علماً آخر سوى أنه يبحث هذا عن أحوال شيء وذلك عن أحوال شيء آخر مغاير له بالذات أو بالاعتبار، فلا يكون تمايز العلوم في أنفسها، وبالنظر إلى ذواتها إلا موضعها وبالجملة فقد أطبقوا على امتناع أن يكون شيء واحد موضوعاً لعلمين من غير اعتبار تغاير، بأن يؤخذ في أحدهما مطلقاً، وفي الآخر مقيداً أو يؤخذ في كل منهما مقيداً بقيد آخر، وامتناع أن يكون موضوع علم I say: The consensus of the scholars is that the differentiation of the sciences in themselves is only according to the differentiation of their subjects. So it is appropriate to begin a science by stating its subject to benefit what distinguishes it in terms of essence, after the definition gave the distinction in terms of concept. Also, to know the aspect of unity for the multiplicity that one aims to encompass in summary, so that when one intends to obtain its details, the quest does not turn from what belongs to it to what does not. There is no doubt that the aspect of unity of the issues of a science primarily and essentially is the subject, for in it is their commonality and by it is their unity, as we will detail. The verification of the matter is that when they attempted to know the states of things as much as human capacity allows, which is intended by wisdom, and they made the realities into types and genera and others, like human, animal, and existent, and they researched their specific states and proved them for them with evidence, so there resulted for them acquired propositions whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities; they called them 'issues' (masa'il). And they made each group of them that goes back to one of those things—whether its subject is itself, a part of it, a type of it, or an essential accident of it—a particular science, distinguished by compilation, naming, and teaching, considering what that group, despite its multiplicity and the difference of its predicates, has of unity from the aspect of the subject, i.e., commonality in it in the mentioned manner. Then it may be unified from other aspects, like benefit, purpose, and the like. And for it there is taken from some of those aspects what benefits its conception in summary, and insofar as it has unity, it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named object, i.e., that composite conceptual thing, as is said: 'It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated' or 'a science of such-and-such rules'; otherwise it is a description (rasm), as is said: 'It is a science by which one is empowered to do such-and-such' or 'by which one guards against such-and-such', or 'it is a tool for such-and-such'. Thus it became clear that the subject is the aspect of unity of the issues of a single science in terms of their essence, even if they have other aspects like definition and purpose. For there is no meaning to one thing being a science and another being a different science except that this one investigates the states of one thing and that one investigates the states of another thing different from it essentially or by consideration. So the differentiation of the sciences in themselves and with respect to their essences is only according to the subject, even if they are differentiated for the student through their definitions, purposes, and the like. For this reason they made the divergence (tabayun), proportionality (tanasub), and overlap (tadakhul) of sciences also according to the subject, meaning that if the subject of one of two sciences is completely divergent from the subject of the other, then the two sciences are absolutely divergent; if it is more general than it, then they are overlapping; and if their subject is one thing essentially but differs by consideration, or two things sharing in a genus or otherwise, then the two sciences are proportional, with details mentioned in their place. In summary, they have agreed on the impossibility of one thing being the subject of two sciences without consideration of difference, such as taking it absolute in one and restricted in the other, or taking it in each restricted by another restriction; and the impossibility of the subject of one science being two things without consideration of their unity in genus, purpose, or otherwise, for there is no meaning to the unity and difference of a science without that.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument by al-Taftazani explaining why the subject (mawdu') is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues. He begins by stating the consensus that sciences are differentiated by their subjects, so it is fitting to begin a science by stating its subject. The subject provides both conceptual and essential distinction, and also reveals the aspect of unity among the diverse issues of that science, preventing the student from straying into unrelated matters. He then verifies the matter by describing the process of scientific inquiry: scholars study the states of things (realities) and obtain propositions (issues) whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities. They then group these issues under a single subject (whether the subject itself, its part, its type, or its essential accident) and designate that group as a particular science. The unity of these issues under the subject is primary, though other unities (like benefit or purpose) may also be considered. The subject may be used to define the science (hadd) if it indicates the reality of the science, or to describe it (rasm) if it indicates the power or purpose. He concludes that the differentiation of sciences in themselves is only by the subject, even though students may differentiate them by definitions or purposes. This leads to the classification of sciences as divergent, overlapping, or proportional based on the relationship between their subjects. Finally, he notes the consensus that a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some consideration of difference, and a single science cannot have two subjects without some unifying consideration.

Key Terms
تباين (tabayun)
تداخل (tadakhul)
تناسب (tanasub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two sciences can share the exact same subject without any difference.
Correction: Al-Taftazani states it is impossible for one thing to be the subject of two sciences without some consideration of difference (e.g., absolute vs. restricted).

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, when are two sciences considered overlapping (mutaqadakhilan)?
A) When their subjects are completely different
B) When the subject of one is more general than the subject of the other
C) When their subjects are identical
D) When they share a common genus

Key Takeaway
The relationships between sciences are determined by the relationships between their subjects; a single subject cannot belong to two sciences without some distinction, and a single science cannot have two subjects without some unifying consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own studies of Islamic knowledge, do you ever treat different subjects (like tafsir and fiqh) as if they are completely separate, or do you see them as overlapping or proportional? How does al-Taftazani's classification challenge you to reconsider the unity of knowledge you seek?
?
Consider the subject of your life's pursuit: is it singular or multiple? If multiple, what unifying purpose ties them together? This lesson warns against a science having two unrelated subjects—what does this say about your own intellectual focus? Are you wasting effort by not identifying the true subject of your learning?
?
When you approach a new topic, do you first ask 'What is its subject?' and how it relates to other things you know? If not, what confusion might result? Commit to starting every study session by clarifying the subject and its relation to other sciences.
