Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Salaf's criticism of kalam means that all forms of kalam are blameworthy.
Correction: The author clarifies that Salaf criticism is limited to cases where kalam is used for sectarian partisanship, corrupting the beliefs of beginners, or promoting philosophical errors. Proper kalam, used to defend creed with certain proofs, is praiseworthy.

Checkpoint

B) They apply only when kalam is used for sectarian partisanship and corrupting beginners

D) They apply only to kalam that uses philosophical arguments

Key Takeaway
Kalam is the noblest science because it deals with the highest subject, has the most sublime object and goal, is essential for other religious sciences, and uses certain proofs, and Salaf criticism only applies to its misuse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Salaf criticized kalam when used for partisanship or pride. Where in your own discussions about religion do you detect a desire to 'win' rather than seek truth? Will you commit to checking your intention before every discussion?
?
This lesson asserts that kalam's proofs are certain and agreed upon by reason and revelation. Is there any belief you hold that you cannot defend with such certainty? What is one step you can take to strengthen that foundation, starting today?

Subject Matter of Kalam: Existent qua Existent
Definition of kalam's subject and its distinction from philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early theologians' definition of kalam's subject matter as 'existent qua existent' and how it is distinguished from philosophy by following Islamic law.

Core Idea
Kalam's subject is existent qua existent, but under Islamic law, not philosophy.

The early theologians of Islam defined the subject matter of kalam as 'existent qua existent'—the most general category. However, they distinguished kalam from philosophy by grounding its inquiries in definitive religious knowledge (qanun al-Islam). This passage from the matn establishes that foundational distinction, showing that kalam is not free rational speculation but a discipline bound by revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of subject matter
Read the first part of the passage focusing on what subject matter is and how it is defined.
Notice the phrase 'الموجود من حيث هو'.
2
Identify the distinction from philosophy
Find the sentence that explains how kalam differs from philosophy. Look for 'يتميز عن الإلهي'.
The key is the phrase 'على قانون الإسلام'.
3
Understand the examples of definitive religious knowledge
The passage gives examples like emanation of multiplicity from One, descent of angels, etc. Reflect on why these are considered definitive.
These are matters known with certainty from religion.
4
Contrast with philosophy's claim
Note that the author says kalam does not follow what is true even if one claims participation with philosophy.
This emphasizes the boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من (قال) والمتقدمون على أن موضوعه الموجود من حيث هو، ويتميز عن الإلهي بكون البحث فيه على قانون الإسلام أي ما علم قطعاً من الدين كصدور الكثرة عن الواحد ونزول الملك من السماء وكون العالم محفوفاً بالعدم والفناء إلى غير ذلك مما تجزم به الملة دون الفلسفة لا ما هو الحق ولو ادعاء لمشاركة الفلسفة ككلام المخالف. أقول : أخر هذه المباحث مع تعلقها بالموضوع محافظة على انتظام الكلام في بيان الموضوع والمسائل والغاية فالمتقدمون من علماء الكلام جعلوا موضوعه الموجود بما هو موجود لرجوع مباحثه إليه على ما قال الإمام حجة الإسلام أن المتكلم ينظر في أعم الأشياء وهو الموجود فيقسمه إلى قديم ومحدث، والمحدث إلى جوهر وعرض والعرض إلى ما يشترط فيه الحياة كالعلم والقدرة وإلى ما لا يشترط كاللون والطعم ويقسم الجوهر إلى الحيوان والنبات والجماد، ويبين أن اختلافها بالأنواع أو بالأعراض، وينظر في القديم فيتبين أنه لا يتكثر ولا يتركب وأنه يتميز عن المحدث بصفات تجب له، وأمور تمتنع عليه، وأحكام تجوز في حقه من غير وجوب أو امتناع، ويبين أن أصل
From (he said): And the early (theologians) held that its subject matter is existent qua existent, and it is distinguished from (the study of) the divine by the fact that the inquiry in it follows the law of Islam, i.e., what is known definitively from religion, such as the emanation of multiplicity from the One, the descent of the angel from heaven, and that the world is surrounded by non-existence and annihilation, and other things that the religious community affirms with certainty, unlike philosophy, not as what is true even if one claims participation with philosophy like the speech of the opponent. I say: He (the author) postponed these discussions despite their connection to the subject matter to maintain order in the exposition of subject matter, issues, and goal. So the early theologians made its subject matter existent qua existent because its investigations reduce to it, as stated by the Imam, the Proof of Islam (al-Ghazali), that the mutakallim looks at the most general thing, namely the existent, and divides it into eternal and originated, and the originated into substance and accident, and the accident into what requires life like knowledge and power and what does not require like color and taste, and divides substance into animal, plant, and mineral, and shows that their differences are by species or accidents, and looks at the eternal and shows that it is not many nor composite, and that it is distinguished from the originated by attributes that are necessary for it, matters that are impossible for it, and rulings that are possible for it without necessity or impossibility, and shows that the origin...
Source pages p. 176

Commentary
This passage discusses the subject matter (mawdu') of ilm al-kalam according to the early theologians (al-mutaqaddimun). The author quotes the matn stating that they held the subject to be 'existent qua existent' (al-mawjud min haythu huwa), but distinct from philosophical metaphysics because the inquiry follows Islamic law (qanun al-Islam) and relies on definitive religious knowledge (ma quti'a bihi min al-din) such as the emanation of multiplicity from God, the descent of angels, and the createdness of the world. The author then explains that the discussion of certain topics is postponed for pedagogical order. He then summarizes al-Ghazali's method: the mutakallim begins with the most general concept ('existent'), divides it into eternal (qadim) and originated (muhdath), further divides the originated into substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad), and accidents into those requiring life (like knowledge and power) and those that do not (like color and taste). Substance is divided into animal, plant, and mineral. Then the mutakallim examines the eternal and establishes that it is not multiple or composite, and is distinguished from the originated by necessary attributes, impossible matters, and possible rulings. The passage cuts off but conveys the systematic method.

Key Terms
موضوع
الموجود بما هو موجود
الإلهي
قانون الإسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam's subject is the same as philosophy's subject of metaphysics.
Correction: Kalam's subject is existent qua existent but under the authority of Islamic law, not rational philosophy.

Checkpoint
According to the early theologians, how is kalam distinguished from philosophy?
A) By using only rational arguments
B) By making inquiry follow Islamic law
C) By rejecting the concept of substance
D) By focusing only on the eternal

Key Takeaway
The early theologians defined kalam's subject as 'existent qua existent' but bound by Islamic law, distinguishing it from philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to understand your faith, do you submit your intellect to what Allah has definitively revealed, or do you allow your own reasoning to override it? What would change in your life if you truly made Islamic law the ultimate judge of your beliefs?
?
The early theologians insisted that kalam must follow 'what is known definitively from religion'—not what seems true to human reason. Are there areas of your faith where you are tempted to rationalize away clear teachings because they clash with modern assumptions? How will you choose to respond the next time that tension arises?
?
This lesson distinguishes between seeking truth for its own sake (philosophy) and seeking truth as defined by revelation (kalam). In your daily life, do you prioritize knowing Allah through His Book and the way of His Prophet, or do you let your own preferences shape your beliefs? What practical step can you take today to align your heart more fully with the 'law of Islam'?

Al-Ghazali's Method: Systematic Division of the Existent
Al-Ghazali's systematic method for kalam: division of the existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's method for kalam: starting from the most general concept of existence and dividing it into categories to derive theological truths.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's method divides existence into eternal/originated, substance/accident, and more.

After establishing the subject matter, the author presents the method of al-Ghazali (the Proof of Islam) for conducting kalam inquiry. This method begins with the most general concept—the existent—and systematically divides it into categories, eventually leading to conclusions about the eternal and its attributes. This passage outlines that systematic approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the description of al-Ghazali's method
Read the passage carefully, focusing on the steps al-Ghazali takes.
Start from 'أن المتكلم ينظر في أعم الأشياء'.
2
Follow the divisions: existent to eternal/originated
Note the first division of the existent into eternal (qadim) and originated (muhdath).
This is a fundamental binary.
3
Trace the subdivisions of originated
The originated is further divided into substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad), and then accidents and substances are subdivided.
Memorize the categories.
4
Understand the analysis of the eternal
The eternal is examined for necessity, impossibility, and possibility.
The passage is cut off but shows the direction.
5
Note the incompleteness
The Arabic ends mid-sentence; the method likely continues.
The translator notes the truncation.

Source Text

This passage discusses the subject matter (mawdu') of ilm al-kalam according to the early theologians (al-mutaqaddimun). The author quotes the matn stating that they held the subject to be 'existent qua existent' (al-mawjud min haythu huwa), but distinct from philosophical metaphysics because the inquiry follows Islamic law (qanun al-Islam) and relies on definitive religious knowledge (ma quti'a bihi min al-din) such as the emanation of multiplicity from God, the descent of angels, and the createdness of the world. The author then explains that the discussion of certain topics is postponed for pedagogical order. He then summarizes al-Ghazali's method: the mutakallim begins with the most general concept ('existent'), divides it into eternal (qadim) and originated (muhdath), further divides the originated into substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad), and accidents into those requiring life (like knowledge and power) and those that do not (like color and taste). Substance is divided into animal, plant, and mineral. Then the mutakallim examines the eternal and establishes that it is not multiple or composite, and is distinguished from the originated by necessary attributes, impossible matters, and possible rulings. The passage cuts off but conveys the systematic method.

B) Eternal and originated
C) Animal and plant
D) Knowledge and power

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's method begins with the most general concept (existent) and systematically divides it to analyze the eternal and originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Where in your daily life do you overlook the signs of the Creator because you haven't properly categorized what is originated and what is eternal? Commit to one specific moment today where you will pause and consciously recognize an originated thing and then reflect on the Eternal.
?
The passage says that the mutakallim shows that the eternal is not many or composite. This means that your mind cannot conceive of God as having parts or being multiple. Does your heart truly affirm the oneness of God in every aspect of your worship, or are there areas where you subconsciously associate partners with Him? Identify one subtle form of shirk in your intentions or actions and resolve to purify it before your next prayer.
?
The division of accidents into those requiring life (like knowledge and power) and those not (like color) demonstrates that some attributes are tied to life. You possess knowledge and power as temporary accidents. Are you using these gifts to know the Eternal, or are you wasting them on the ephemeral? What is one specific way you can use your knowledge today to draw closer to Allah, and how will you ensure it is not just for show?

The Law of Islam: Method, Not Truth
Clarifies that adherence to Islamic rules is by intention, not actual truth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the phrase 'law of Islam' in the definition of kalam refers to a methodological commitment to Islamic principles, not a claim that every statement is objectively true.

Core Idea
The 'law of Islam' is about method, not about being right.

The passage you are about to read tackles a subtle but crucial point: when we say kalam is defined by adhering to the 'law of Islam', do we mean that every argument it produces is actually true? Or does the phrase describe a way of reasoning? Al-Sanusi argues for the latter, and shows why the alternative interpretations would make the definition useless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice how the author sets up the problem: if 'law of Islam' meant actual truth, the definition would exclude many recognized schools of kalam.
Pay attention to the examples: corporealists, Muʿtazilites, Kharijites.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The passage considers two possible meanings for 'law of Islam': (1) what is actually true and truly Islamic, and (2) what the researcher believes to be true. See how each leads to a problem.
The first interpretation fails because every claimant says they are true. The second fails because philosophy also shares it.
3
Understand the correct interpretation
The author implies that the correct meaning is a methodological adherence: preserving legal rules and certainties in the process of investigation, not a guarantee of truth in every result.
This is consistent with the earlier definition of kalam as relying on Islamic principles.
4
Reflect on the objection
The objection about circularity or insufficiency is resolved once we see that the definition is about method, not truth. The definition does not need to prove its own correctness at every step.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فحاصله أن يحافظ في جميع المباحث على القواعد الشرعية ولا يخالف القطعيات منها جرياً على مقتضى نظر العقول الفاصرة على ما هو قانون الفلسفة، لا أن يكون جميع المباحث حقة في نفس الأمر منتسبة إلى الإسلام بالتحقيق، وإلا لما صدق التعريف على كلام الجسمة والمعتزلة والخوارج ومن يجري مجراهم وعلى هذا لا يرد الاعتراض بأن قانون الإسلام ما هو الحق من مسائل الكلام فإن أريد الحقية والانتساب إلى الإسلام بحسب الواقع لم يصلح هذا القيد لتميز الكلام عن غيره، لأنه ليس لازماً بيناً، إذ كل من المتكلم وغيره يدعي حقية مقاله، ولم يصدق التعريف على كلام المخالف لبطلان كثير من أنه كلام وفاقاً، وإن أريد بحسب اعتقاد الباحث حقاً كان أو باطلاً، لم يتميز الكلام بهذا القيد عن الإلهي لاشتراكهما في ذلك.
In short, its gist is that one should preserve in all investigations the legal rules and not contradict the certainties among them, following the dictate of sound intellects as is the law of philosophy, not that all investigations are true in themselves and actually attributed to Islam, otherwise the definition would not apply to the speech of the corporealists, the Muʿtazilites, the Kharijites, and those who follow their path. And on this basis, the objection that the law of Islam is what is true in kalam questions does not arise. For if truth and attribution to Islam are intended according to reality, this restriction is not sufficient to distinguish kalam from others, because it is not a necessary and evident condition, since every theologian and non-theologian claims his statement is true, and the definition would not apply to the speech of the opponent because much of it is false, even though it is kalam by agreement. And if it is intended according to the belief of the researcher, whether true or false, then kalam is not distinguished by this restriction from divine philosophy, since they share that.
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage clarifies the meaning of 'law of Islam' and answers a potential objection. The author says the essence of the definition is to preserve the legal rules in all investigations and not contradict the certainties, following sound reason—but this is a methodological description, not a claim that all results are true. If it meant the latter, the definition would exclude the speech of corporealists, Muʿtazilites, and Kharijites, which is agreed to be part of kalam. Then the objection is stated: if 'law of Islam' means what is actually true, it fails to distinguish kalam from non-kalam because every claimant says they are true. Also, it would exclude false statements that are still kalam. If it means what the researcher believes, then both kalam and philosophy share this, so the distinction collapses. The implied answer is that the definition's intention is the researcher's adherence to Islamic principles in method, not the truth of the conclusions.

Misconception: One might think that the definition of kalam requires every statement to be objectively true and authentically Islamic.
Correction: The definition is about a methodological commitment to Islamic principles in reasoning, not a guarantee that all conclusions are correct.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The 'law of Islam' in kalam's definition is a methodological commitment, not a claim of objective truth; this resolves the objection that the definition is circular or insufficient.

Objection to the Subject of Kalam and Its Answer
Sanusi answers an objection by classifying topics as principles or corollaries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that kalam discusses non-existent things is resolved by classifying topics as principles or corollaries.

Core Idea
Kalam's subject is the existent; non-existents are principles or corollaries.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a key objection to the definition of kalam's subject as 'the existent as such.' Critics from al-Mawāqif argue that kalam investigates things like inquiry (naẓar) and the non-existent (maʿdūm), which do not fit that definition. Sanusi provides a clear response by distinguishing between the core problems, principles, and corollaries. This lesson unpacks that objection and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the objection raised against defining kalam's subject as the existent. Note the four items mentioned: inquiry, proof, non-existent, and state.
The objector claims these items do not fit the subject definition.
2
Identify the problematic items
The objector lists two categories: things not considered existent (like inquiry and proof) and things with no existence at all (like non-existent and state). Recognize why these challenge the subject definition.
3
Examine the answer
Sanusi's answer has two parts: inquiry and proof are principles (mabādiʾ) of kalam, not problems (masāʾil); non-existent and state are corollaries (lawāḥiq) of the problem of existence.
This preserves the subject definition by moving these discussions outside the core problems.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this classification defends the integrity of kalam's subject and shows the importance of distinguishing between principles, problems, and corollaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : فإن قيل : قد يبحث مع نفي الوجود الذهني عن أحوال ما لا يعتبر وجوده كالنظر والدليل وما لا وجود كالمعدوم والحال قلنا ولواحق ولو سلم فنفي الذهني رأي البعض). أقول : اعترض في المواقف على كون موضوع الكلام هو الموجود من حيث هو بأنه قد يبحث عن أحوال ما لا يعتبر وجوده، وإن كان موجوداً كالنظر والدليل وعن أحوال ما لا وجود له أصلاً كالمعدوم والحال ولا يجوز أن يؤخذ الموجود أعم من الذهني والخارجي ليعم الكل لأن المتكلمين لا يقولون بالوجود الذهني، والجواب : إنا لا نسلم كون هذه المباحث من مسائل الكلام بل مباحث النظر والدليل من مباديه على ما قررنا وبحث المعدوم والحال من لواحق مسألة الوجود.
He said: For if it is said: 'Despite denying mental existence, one investigates the states of what is not considered existent, like speculative inquiry and proof, and what has no existence, like the non-existent and the state,' we say: 'They are corollaries, and even if granted, the denial of mental existence is the opinion of some.' I say: An objection was raised in al-Mawāqif against the idea that the subject of kalam is the existent qua existent, because it investigates the states of what is not considered existent, even if it is existent, like inquiry and proof, and the states of what has no existence at all, like the non-existent and the state. It is not permissible to take the existent to include mental and external existence to cover all, because the theologians do not affirm mental existence. The answer: We do not concede that these investigations are among the problems (masāʾil) of kalam; rather, the investigations of inquiry and proof are among its principles (mabādiʾ) as we have established, and the investigation of the non-existent and the state is among the corollaries (lawāḥiq) of the problem of existence.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and answer regarding the subject of kalam. The objection, from al-Mawāqif, argues that if kalam's subject is the existent as such, then it should not discuss things that are not considered existent, such as speculative inquiry (naẓar), proof (dalīl), the non-existent (maʿdūm), and the state (ḥāl). The objector also notes that theologians deny mental existence, so the subject cannot be extended to include mental existence. The answer is twofold: first, discussions of inquiry and proof are not part of the problems (masāʾil) of kalam but are its principles (mabādiʾ). Second, discussions of the non-existent and the state are corollaries (lawāḥiq) of the problem of existence. Thus, they do not violate the subject definition.

المعدوم
الحال

الدليل
المبادئ
اللواحق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think any discussion of non-existent things invalidates the subject definition of kalam.
Correction: These discussions are either principles or corollaries, so they do not undermine the subject.

Checkpoint

B) By saying they are principles or corollaries
C) By accepting mental existence

Key Takeaway
Kalam's subject remains the existent, as discussions of non-existents are either foundational principles or derived corollaries.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Principles of Kalam Need Not Be Self-Evident
Author answers objection that principles of kalam must be self-evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the principles of kalam do not need to be self-evident and how they can be established in a lower science without circularity.

Core Idea
Kalam's principles can be proven in a lower science, not in a circle.

The objection claims that since there is no higher religious science above kalam, its principles must be self-evident. Sanusi answers by clarifying that principles can be explained within the science itself or in a lower science, and gives the analogy of usul al-fiqh relying on Arabic grammar. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection: 'Its principles must be self-evident, since there is no religious science above it.' Understand the reasoning behind the challenge.
The objector assumes that all principles must come from a higher science or be self-evident.
2
Read the answer
Now read Sanusi's answer: principles can be clarified within the science itself or in a lower science. Pay attention to the phrase 'not in a circular manner' – this is key.
Circularity (dawr) would be invalid, so the answer carefully avoids it.
3
Examine the example
Sanusi gives an example: the principles of religious law are established in non-religious sciences like Arabic grammar. Think about how usul al-fiqh depends on Arabic language rules.
This shows that kalam can similarly rely on a 'lower' science like logic or epistemology.
4
Reflect on circularity
Why does this answer avoid circularity? Because the lower science does not depend on kalam for its own principles. The support is one-way.
This preserves the hierarchy of sciences without requiring self-evident principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : مبادئه يجب أن تكون بينة بنفسها إذ ليس فوقه علم شرعي. قلنا: قد تبين مبادىء العلم فيه أو في علم أدنى، لا على وجه الدور ومبادىء الشرعي في غير الشرعي، كالأصول في العربية .
For it is said: Its principles must be self-evident, since there is no religious science above it. We say: The principles of a science may be clarified within it or in a lower science, not in a circular manner. And the principles of religious law are [established] in non-religious sciences, like usul in Arabic grammar.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding the principles of kalam. The objector argues that the principles (mabadi') of kalam must be self-evident (bayyina bi nafsiha) because there is no religious science above kalam from which they could be derived. The author responds by pointing out that the principles of a science can be established either within that science itself or in a lower science (i.e., a more basic science), without circularity (dawr). As an example, the principles of religious law (shar'i) are established in non-religious sciences, such as the principles of jurisprudence (usul) being established in Arabic grammar (al-'arabiyya). Thus, it is not required that kalam's principles be self-evident; they can be proven in another discipline.

Key Terms
المبادئ
العلم الشرعي
الدور
الأصول في العربية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All principles of a science must be self-evident if there is no higher science above it.
Correction: Principles can be established in a lower science (or within the science itself), as shown by the example of usul in Arabic grammar.

Checkpoint

A) Only in kalam itself.
B) In a lower science.
C) In a higher science.
D) In revelation.

Key Takeaway
The principles of a science may be established in a lower science, avoiding circularity.

How Kalam Borrows Its Principles: Answering the Objection
Sanusi answers the objection about Kalam's principles by classifying how principles are clarified across sciences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Kalam's principles need not be self-evident and how they can be established from other sciences without circularity.

Core Idea
Kalam's principles are clarified in other sciences, not necessarily higher ones.

Before delving into the content of Kalam, one must first address a fundamental objection: if Kalam is the highest religious science, how can it have non-self-evident principles? In this passage, Sanusi presents and defeats this objection by explaining the relationship between sciences and their principles. He shows that principles can be clarified in a higher or even a lower science, as long as circularity is avoided.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. Understand the objector's claim that Kalam cannot have non-self-evident principles because there is no higher religious science.
The objector assumes that principles must be clarified in a higher science, which would be impossible for Kalam.
2
Study the author's response
Notice how Sanusi first gives a counterexample from Usul al-Fiqh borrowing from Arabic grammar. Then note his detailed classification of principles.
The response does not reject the need for clarification but shows where it can occur.
3
Distinguish the three cases
The author describes three possibilities for principles: self-evident, clarified in a higher science, or clarified in a lower science. Identify the examples given for each.
Each case has a different logical relationship between sciences.
4
Consider the circularity condition
Pay attention to the condition that principles clarified in a lower science must be proven without relying on the higher science to avoid circularity.
This ensures that the lower science can stand independently when establishing the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: لا يجوز أن يكون للكلام مبادىء تفتقر إلى البيان وتثبت بالبرهان، لأن مبادىء العلم إنما تتبين في علم منه، وليس في العلوم الشرعية ما هو أعلى من الكلام، بل الكل جزئي بالنسبة إليه، ومتوقف بالآخرة عليه، فمبادئه لا تكون إلا بينة بنفسها. قلنا: ما يبين فيه مبادىء العلم الشرعي لا يجب أن يكون علماً أعلى، ولا أن يكون علماً شرعياً، للإطباق على أن علم الأصول يستمد من العربية ويبين فيها بعض مبادئه، وتفصيل ذلك على ما هو المذكور في الشفاء وغيره: مبادىء العلم قد تكون بينة بنفسها فلا تبين في علم أصلاً، وقد تكون غير بينة فتبين في علم أعلى بجلالة محله عن أن يبين في ذلك العلم، كقولنا: الجسم مؤلف من الهيولي والصورة فإنه من مبادىء الطبيعي، ومن مسائل الفلسفة الأولى، أو في علم أدنى لدنو شأنه عن أن يبين في ذلك العلم، كامتناع الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ فإنه من مسائل الطبيعي ومن مبادىء الإلهي لإثبات الهيولي والصورة فيجب أن يبين بمقدمات لا تتوقف صحتها عليها لئلا يلزم الدور.
If it is said: It is not permissible that speculative theology have principles that are in need of clarification and are established by demonstration, because the principles of a science are only clarified in a [higher] science, and there is no science among the religious sciences higher than kalām; rather, all [other sciences] are subordinate to it and ultimately dependent upon it, so its principles can only be self-evident (bayyina bi-nafsihā). We say: That in which the principles of a religious science are clarified need not be a higher science, nor need it be a religious science, because of the agreement that the science of uṣūl (principles of fiqh) derives from Arabic grammar and some of its principles are clarified therein. The details of this, according to what is mentioned in the Shifāʾ and elsewhere, are: the principles of a science may be self-evident, in which case they are not clarified in any science; or they may be non-self-evident, in which case they are clarified either in a higher science because of the exaltedness of its subject matter from being clarified in the lower science — as in our statement: 'The body is composed of hyle (hayūlā) and form,' which is among the principles of physics and among the topics of first philosophy (metaphysics); or in a lower science because of the lowliness of its status from being clarified in the higher science — such as the impossibility of the indivisible part (al-juzʾ alladhī lā yatajazzaʾ), which is a topic of physics and a principle of metaphysics for proving hyle and form, so it must be demonstrated by premises whose soundness does not depend on it, to avoid circularity (dawr).
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage answers an objection about the principles of Kalam. The objector argues that Kalam cannot have non-self-evident principles because it is the highest religious science; therefore its principles must be self-evident. The author responds by clarifying that principles need not be clarified in a higher science, nor must they be self-evident. He gives the example of Uṣūl al-Fiqh borrowing from Arabic grammar. Then he elaborates: principles can be self-evident (bayyina bi-nafsihā) and thus need no clarification; or they can be non-self-evident and clarified in either a higher science (e.g., the composition of body from hyle and form is a principle of physics but a topic of metaphysics) or a lower science (e.g., the impossibility of an indivisible part is a topic of physics but a principle of metaphysics). The lower science provides premises that do not depend on the higher science, avoiding circularity (dawr). Thus, Kalam can legitimately have principles that are proven in other sciences.

Key Terms
مبادىء
بيان
برهان
بينة بنفسها
دور
الهيولي
الصورة
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A science cannot borrow principles from a lower science.
Correction: The passage shows that principles can be clarified in a lower science as long as the premises do not rely on the higher science, thus avoiding circularity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, in which science is the principle 'the body is composed of hyle and form' clarified?
A) In a lower science (physics)
B) In a higher science (metaphysics)
C) In the same science (Kalam)
D) It is self-evident and needs no clarification

Key Takeaway
Kalam's principles can be clarified in other sciences, higher or lower, provided circularity is avoided.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a principle of faith that is not immediately clear to you (like the nature of the soul or divine attributes), do you dismiss it because you think religious truths must be self-evident? What would it mean for you to humbly seek clarification from other sources of knowledge, even those you consider 'lower' like science or logic, as the ulema did?
?
Sanusi uses the example of Usul al-Fiqh borrowing from Arabic grammar. Reflect: In your own learning of deen, are you willing to learn from seemingly 'lower' disciplines (grammar, logic, natural sciences) to better understand your faith, or do you feel they are beneath the dignity of religious study? How does that affect your depth of understanding?
?
The passage warns against circularity: when using a lower science to clarify a principle, the premises must not already depend on the principle being clarified. Consider your own reasoning about religious matters: do you ever find yourself arguing in circles, assuming what you set out to prove? How can you ensure your search for truth is sound and not self-referential?

Establishing the First Principle in Kalam
Taftazani explains the rule for establishing a science's first principle and applies it to kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first principle of kalam is established and why it requires proof from another science.

Core Idea
Kalam's first principle cannot be proven within kalam itself.

In this passage, Taftazani explains a key methodological rule: a science sometimes proves its own first principle, but only under specific conditions to avoid circularity. He then applies this rule to the science of kalam, showing that its first principle—the investigation of the states of possible things—must be established by a decisive proof from a lower or higher science, even if that science is not considered Islamic.

Guided Path
1
Read the rule and condition
Read the passage carefully. Note the general statement that a principle is not normally proven within its own science, with an exception if it is not a principle for all issues and is proven by an issue that depends on it.
The author uses the phrase 'to avoid circularity' (li'alla yadūr).
2
Understand the examples
Identify the examples from geometry and usul al-fiqh. Notice how something can be a principle in one respect and an issue in another.
For example, 'command for obligation' is an issue in usul al-fiqh, but it relies on the principle of qiyas derived from the Qur'an.
3
Apply to kalam
See why kalam's own investigation—'the states of possible things not from the perspective of dependence'—does not fit the exception. Therefore, its principle must come from outside.
Taftazani says 'this is not of that kind' (la yakūn min hādhā al-qabīl).
4
Conclude with the source of proof
The principle is established by a decisive proof from a lower science (like natural philosophy) or a higher science (like metaphysics), even if that science is not deemed Islamic.
This shows kalam's reliance on philosophical foundations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يبين (وقد يبين في ذلك العلم نفسه بشرط أن لا يكون مبدأ لجميع مسائله، بمسألة تتوقف عليه لئلا يدور) . وأن ، فهذا يكون مبدأ باعتبار ومسألة باعتبار كأكثر مسائل الهندسة وككون الأمر للوجوب فإنه مسألة من الأصول، ومبدأ المسألة وجوب القياس تمسكاً بقوله تعالى : فاعتبروا ولا يخفى أنه يجب في هذا القسم أن يكون بحثاً عن أحوال موضوع الصناعة، ليصح كونه من مسائلها فما نحن فيه أعني البحث عن أحوال الممكنات لا على وجه الاستناد لا يكون من هذا القبيل فتعين البيان في علم أدنى أو أعلى فيثبت هذا المبدأ بدليل قطعي من غير مخالفة للقواعد الشرعية، وإن لم يعد ذلك العلم من العلوم الإسلامية، كالإلهي الباحث عن أحوال الموجود على الإطلاق.
It is not explained (though it may be explained in that science itself on condition that it is not a principle for all its issues, by an issue that depends on it, to avoid circularity). And so, this is a principle in one respect and an issue in another, like most geometrical issues, and like the command for obligation being an issue in uṣūl (principles of jurisprudence) while the principle of the issue is the obligation of qiyās (analogical reasoning) by adhering to His statement: 'So take heed' (Qur'an 59:2). And it is not hidden that in this section the investigation must be about the states of the subject matter of the craft, in order that it be valid as one of its issues. Thus our case, namely the investigation of the states of possible things, not from the perspective of dependence, is not of this kind. So the clarification is determined in a lower or higher science, and this principle is established by a decisive proof, without violating the legal principles, even if that science is not counted among the Islamic sciences, like the metaphysical science that investigates the states of existent absolutely.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation about how a first principle (mabda') of a science can be established. The author begins by stating a rule: a principle is not generally proven within the science itself, except when it is not a principle for all issues but only for some, and it is proven by an issue that depends on it, to avoid circularity. He gives examples: in geometry, many issues are also principles in other respects; similarly, in uṣūl al-fiqh, the command for obligation is an issue while the obligation of qiyās is a principle derived from the verse 'So take heed'. Then he turns to the science of kalam: our investigation here is of the states of possible things (al-mumkināt) not from the perspective of their dependence on God, but from a more general perspective. This kind of investigation cannot be proven within kalam itself. Therefore, the first principle must be established by a decisive proof from a lower science (like natural philosophy) or a higher science (like metaphysics), even if that science is not considered an Islamic science, such as metaphysics that investigates existence as such. The point is that kalam's own subject matter requires external justification.

A) When it is a principle for all issues
B) When it is not a principle for all issues and is proven by an issue depending on it
C) When it is a matter of divine attributes
D) When it is a principle of geometry

Key Takeaway
The first principle of kalam requires external proof from a lower or higher science because it cannot be established within kalam itself without circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the foundations of your faith, what is your 'first principle'—the most basic belief you hold? Is it proven by something outside of your own reasoning, or are you relying on circular logic without realizing it? This lesson shows that even the science of kalam must look outside itself for its starting point. Where do you look for yours, and is your search as rigorous as Taftazani demands?
?
Taftazani warns against circularity: using something that depends on a principle to prove that very principle. Reflect on your own beliefs. Can you identify any areas where you argue in a circle—believing something because of a reason that itself depends on that belief? Will you commit to seeking a decisive proof from outside for that belief today?
?
The passage requires that the first principle of kalam be established by a decisive proof from a higher or lower science, even if that science is not Islamic. This demands humility—recognizing that your own discipline cannot be self-sufficient. Is there an area in your life where you refuse to seek external validation because of pride? How might accepting help from outside (a teacher, scripture, or a different field of knowledge) actually strengthen your convictions?

The Specific Focus of Kalam: Investigating Possible Things in Relation to God
Taftazani clarifies the precise meaning of investigating possible things in kalam from Sharh al-Saha'if.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what Taftazani means by investigating the states of possible things from the perspective of their dependence on God.

Core Idea
Kalam does not study all things, only those that depend on God.

This passage from Taftazani's commentary refines our understanding of the subject matter of kalam. It clarifies that the discipline does not examine every possible state of contingent beings, but only those states that arise from their need for and dependence on God. This distinction is crucial for defining the scope of theological inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first negation, then positive definition.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms 'investigation of the states of possible things' and 'from the perspective of dependence'. Understand how they are defined in the passage.
3
Understand the correction
Note that the passage corrects a misunderstanding: the perspective of dependence is not considered in every issue, but defines the subject matter.
4
Grasp the positive definition
Understand that the states investigated are those that flow from God's power and arise from the creatures' need for Him.
This connects the subject matter to God's power and the creature's dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهنا شيء آخر وهو أن المفهوم من شرح الصحائف أن ليس معنى البحث عن أحوال الممكنات على وجه الاستناد أن يكون ذلك ملاحظاً في جميع المسائل، بل أن يكون البحث عن أحوال تعرض للممكنات من جهة استنادها إلى الله تعالى : فإن أحوال الممكنات التي يبحث عنها في الكلام أحوال مخصوصة معلومة بحكم فيضانها عن تأثير قدرة الله تعالى، ذلك إنما يكون لحاجتها إلى الله تعالى، فيكون عروضها للممكنات ناشئاً عن جهة حاجتها إليه .
And here is another thing: what is understood from Sharḥ al-Ṣaḥā'if is that the meaning of 'investigation of the states of possible things from the perspective of dependence' is not that this [consideration] is observed in all issues, rather it is that the investigation is about the states that occur to possible things from the aspect of their dependence on God Almighty. For the states of possible things that are investigated in kalām are specific states known by virtue of their flowing from the effect of the power of God Almighty; that is only due to their need for God Almighty, so their occurrence to the possible things arises from the aspect of their need for Him.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a clarification of a key term in kalam methodology. Taftazani quotes from the commentary on al-Ṣaḥā'if to explain what it means to investigate the states of possible things (aḥwāl al-mumkināt) 'from the perspective of dependence' (min wajh al-istinād). He first negates a misunderstanding: it does not mean that this perspective is present in every single issue of kalam. Rather, it means that the investigation concerns those specific states that occur to possible things because of their dependence on God. He further explains that these states are known by the fact that they emanate from God's power (qudra), and they occur because of the possible things' need (ḥāja) for God. So the states are not just any states but those that are caused by and reveal the relationship of creatures to their Creator. This narrows the subject matter of kalam to theological aspects of the contingent world.

Key Terms
أحوال الممكنات
الاستناد
قدرة الله
الحاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that kalam examines all states of possible things equally, without restriction.
Correction: The passage clarifies that kalam only investigates those states that arise from the possible things' dependence on God, not all states.

Checkpoint
According to the clarification from Sharh al-Saha'if, what does 'investigation of possible things from the perspective of dependence' mean?
A) Investigating every state of possible things without restriction
B) Investigating only the states that arise from the possible things' dependence on God
C) Investigating only the states that are independent of God
D) Investigating the states of the soul

Key Takeaway
Kalam does not study all aspects of creation; it focuses specifically on those aspects that reveal creation's dependence on God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you—your health, your wealth, your relationships—do you naturally see them as 'flowing from God's power,' or do you unconsciously treat them as independent realities? What would change in your daily mindset if you actively saw everything as contingent on Him?
?
Taftazani says kalām only investigates states of possible things that arise from their need for God. In your own life, are there areas you examine from a perspective of need for God, or do you analyze them as if they stand alone? What would it mean to bring that 'dependence lens' into a specific struggle you face right now?
?
You have just read that the meaning of investigating things 'from the aspect of dependence' is not that every issue explicitly mentions it, but that the entire discipline is defined by it. Is there a similar defining principle in your relationship with Allah—one that shapes everything you do, even when you are not consciously thinking of it? If not, what is blocking that from being your foundation?

Refuting the Scope Objection: Proving the Creator Belongs to Kalām
Taftazani presents the objection and offers a preliminary refutation regarding the subject of kalām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that proving the Creator cannot be an issue of kalām because the subject is not proven within the science, and Taftazani's refutation affirming it as the highest issue.

Core Idea
Proving the Creator is the highest issue of kalām, not outside its scope.

This lesson addresses a methodological objection: if God's existence is the subject of kalām, how can proving it be an issue within kalām? Taftazani clarifies that the objection misunderstands both the nature of kalām and its relationship to metaphysics. He explains that while later scholars expanded kalām's scope, the core remains focused on divine attributes, prophethood, and eschatology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Let the objection and response flow naturally.
2
Identify the objection and its basis
The objection claims that the subject of a science is not proven within that science. Since God is the subject of kalām, proving His existence would be impossible within kalām.
3
Follow Taftazani's response
Notice that Taftazani first explains why later scholars mixed metaphysical topics into kalām, then insists that proving the Necessary Being is indeed a core issue of kalām.
4
Grasp the refutation
Understand that Taftazani refutes the narrow definition of kalām's subject and affirms that proving the Creator is the highest issue, not outside its scope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واعترض بأن إثبات الصانع من أعلى مطالب الكلام وموضوع العلم لا يبين فيه، بل فيما فوقه حتى ينتهي إلى ما موضوعه بين الوجود كالموجود، من حيث هو أقول: لما كان من المباحث الحكمية ما لا يقدح في العقائد الدينية، ولم يناسب غير الكلام من العلوم الإسلامية، خلطها المتأخرون بمسائل الكلام إفاضة للحقائق، وإفادة لما أن يستعان به في التقصي عن المضايق عسى وإلا فلا نزاع في أن أصل الكلام لا يتجاوز مباحث الذات والصفات والنبوة والإمامة والمعاد، وما يتعلق بذلك من أحوال الممكنات، فلذا اقتصر القوم في إبطال كون موضوع الكلام ذات الله وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من جهة الاستناد على أنه لو كان كذلك لما كان إثباته من مطالب الكلام، لأن موضوع العلم لا يبين فيه، بل في علم أعلى إلى أن ينتهي إلى ما موضوعه بين الثبوت كالموجود، وذلك لأن حقيقية العلم إثبات الأعراض الذاتية للشيء على ما هو معنى الهلية المركبة، ولا خفاء في أنها بعد الهلية البسيطة لأن ما لا يعلم ثبوته لا يطلب ثبوت شيء له . لكن لا نزاع في أن إثبات الواجب بمعنى إقامة البرهان على وجوده من أعلى مطالب الكلام ، ثم كونه مبدأ الممكنات بالاختيار أو الإيجاب بلا وسط في الكل أو بوسط في البعض بحث آخر ـ والقول بأن إثباته إنما هو من مسائل الإلهي دون الكلام ظاهر الفساد، وإلا لكان هو أحد العلوم الإسلامية بل رئيسها، ورأسها ومبنى القواعد الشرعية وأساسها وأجاب بعضهم بأنه جاز ههنا إثبات الموضوع في العلم لوجهتين : الأول : أن الوجود من أعراضه الذاتية لكونه واجب الوجود بخلاف سائر العلوم، فإن الوجود إنما يلحق موضوعاتها لأمر مباين وكان هذا مراد من قال :
He said: An objection is made that proving the Maker is among the highest issues of kalām, yet the subject of a science is not explained in it, but rather in something above it, until it reaches that whose subject is evident in existence, like the existent as such. I say: Since there are among the metaphysical investigations those that do not harm religious beliefs and are not suited to any Islamic science other than kalām, the later [scholars] mixed them with the issues of kalām, in order to pour forth truths and to provide that by which one may seek help in exploring narrow passages, perhaps. Otherwise, there is no dispute that the essence of kalām does not go beyond the discussions of the Essence, attributes, prophethood, imamate, eschatology, and what pertains to that of the states of possible things. Hence the people restricted themselves in refuting that the subject of kalām is the Essence of God alone or with the essence of possible things from the aspect of dependence, on the grounds that if it were so, then its proof would not be among the issues of kalām, because the subject of a science is not explained in it, but in a higher science, until it reaches that whose subject is evident in existence, like the existent. That is because the reality of a science is to prove the essential attributes of a thing according to the meaning of the composite quiddity (al-haythiyya al-murakkaba), and it is not hidden that it is after the simple quiddity (al-haythiyya al-basīṭa), for that whose existence is not known cannot have anything attributed to it. But there is no dispute that proving the Necessary Being in the sense of establishing the proof for His existence is among the highest issues of kalām. Then, His being the principle of possible things by choice or necessity, without an intermediary in all or with an intermediary in some, is another discussion. And the claim that proving Him is only among the issues of metaphysics (al-ilāhī) and not of kalām is clearly corrupt; otherwise, it would not be one of the Islamic sciences, indeed it is the chief of them, their head, the foundation of the legal principles, and their basis. And some answered that it is permissible here to prove the subject in the science for two reasons: First: that existence is among His essential attributes because He is the Necessary Existent, unlike other sciences, for existence only attaches to their subjects from an external cause. And this was the meaning of the one who said:
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding whether proving the existence of the Creator (al-Ṣāni‘) belongs to the issues (maṭālib) of kalam. The objection states that the subject of a science is not proven within that science but in a higher science; since the existence of God is the subject of kalam, proving it cannot be an issue of kalam. Taftazani responds first by noting that later scholars mixed metaphysical discussions into kalam for beneficial purposes, but the core of kalam is limited to discussions of the Essence, attributes, prophethood, imamate, and eschatology, along with states of possible things related to these. The refuters argue that if the subject were God alone or with possible things, then proving God would not be a kalam issue. But Taftazani insists that it is a common issue that proving the Necessary Being is among the highest issues of kalam. He then rejects the claim that it belongs only to metaphysics (al-ilāhī), because kalam is the chief Islamic science. Finally, he presents an answer from some scholars: it is permissible to prove the subject within kalam for two reasons. The first reason is that existence is an essential attribute (ʿaraḍ dhātī) of the Necessary Being, whereas in other sciences existence is accidental to the subject. The text ends mid-sentence at 'وكان هذا مراد من قال:' suggesting the second reason or conclusion is missing in this excerpt.

مطالب الكلام
موضوع العلم
الواجب
الهلية المركبة
الهلية البسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Proving the existence of God is not a topic of kalām because God is the subject of kalām.
Correction: Taftazani refutes this by showing that proving the Necessary Being's existence is indeed the highest issue of kalām, and the subject of kalām is not God alone but includes His attributes and related matters.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, what is the core subject matter of kalām?
A) Proving the Creator
B) The Essence, attributes, prophethood, imamate, and eschatology
C) All metaphysical investigations
D) The possible things

Key Takeaway
Proving the existence of the Creator is a central and highest issue of kalām, despite methodological objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relegated the proof of God to 'philosophy' and considered it optional for your faith? What would you lose by not making the existence of God the central, non-negotiable issue of your Islamic understanding, as Taftazani insists it is the highest issue of kalām?
?
If kalām is the head and basis of all legal principles, are you building your practice of Islam on a foundation you have personally verified? How would your commitment to learning change if you truly believed that your fiqh and iman rest on the proofs of God's existence?
?
The objection suggests that proving God is beyond kalām’s scope. Have you ever avoided deep theological questions because they seemed too technical or unnecessary? What would it take for you to engage with the hardest proofs of God's existence, not as an academic exercise, but as the anchor of your certainty?

The Essential Attribute Argument: Why Proving the Subject is Permissible in Kalām
Some scholars answer that proving the subject is permissible because existence is an essential attribute of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific answer given by some scholars that existence is an essential attribute of the Necessary Being, making it permissible to prove the subject of kalām within the science.

Core Idea
Existence is an essential attribute of God, so proving it is part of kalām.

In this lesson, we examine a refined answer to the objection: it is permissible to prove the subject (God's existence) within kalām because existence is an essential attribute of the Necessary Being. This contrasts with other sciences, where the existence of the subject is taken from outside.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note that it begins with 'And some answered'.
2
Understand the first reason
The passage gives the first reason why proving God's existence is permissible in kalām: existence is an essential attribute of the Necessary Being.
3
Contrast with other sciences
In other sciences, existence attaches to the subject from an external cause; it is not essential. This distinction makes kalām unique.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: واعترض بأن إثبات الصانع من أعلى مطالب الكلام وموضوع العلم لا يبين فيه، بل فيما فوقه حتى ينتهي إلى ما موضوعه بين الوجود كالموجود، من حيث هو أقول: لما كان من المباحث الحكمية ما لا يقدح في العقائد الدينية، ولم يناسب غير الكلام من العلوم الإسلامية، خلطها المتأخرون بمسائل الكلام إفاضة للحقائق، وإفادة لما أن يستعان به في التقصي عن المضايق عسى وإلا فلا نزاع في أن أصل الكلام لا يتجاوز مباحث الذات والصفات والنبوة والإمامة والمعاد، وما يتعلق بذلك من أحوال الممكنات، فلذا اقتصر القوم في إبطال كون موضوع الكلام ذات الله وحده أو مع ذات الممكنات من جهة الاستناد على أنه لو كان كذلك لما كان إثباته من مطالب الكلام، لأن موضوع العلم لا يبين فيه، بل في علم أعلى إلى أن ينتهي إلى ما موضوعه بين الثبوت كالموجود، وذلك لأن حقيقية العلم إثبات الأعراض الذاتية للشيء على ما هو معنى الهلية المركبة، ولا خفاء في أنها بعد الهلية البسيطة لأن ما لا يعلم ثبوته لا يطلب ثبوت شيء له . لكن لا نزاع في أن إثبات الواجب بمعنى إقامة البرهان على وجوده من أعلى مطالب الكلام ، ثم كونه مبدأ الممكنات بالاختيار أو الإيجاب بلا وسط في الكل أو بوسط في البعض بحث آخر ـ والقول بأن إثباته إنما هو من مسائل الإلهي دون الكلام ظاهر الفساد، وإلا لكان هو أحد العلوم الإسلامية بل رئيسها، ورأسها ومبنى القواعد الشرعية وأساسها وأجاب بعضهم بأنه جاز ههنا إثبات الموضوع في العلم لوجهتين : الأول : أن الوجود من أعراضه الذاتية لكونه واجب الوجود بخلاف سائر العلوم، فإن الوجود إنما يلحق موضوعاتها لأمر مباين وكان هذا مراد من قال :
He said: An objection is made that proving the Maker is among the highest issues of kalām, yet the subject of a science is not explained in it, but rather in something above it, until it reaches that whose subject is evident in existence, like the existent as such. I say: Since there are among the metaphysical investigations those that do not harm religious beliefs and are not suited to any Islamic science other than kalām, the later [scholars] mixed them with the issues of kalām, in order to pour forth truths and to provide that by which one may seek help in exploring narrow passages, perhaps. Otherwise, there is no dispute that the essence of kalām does not go beyond the discussions of the Essence, attributes, prophethood, imamate, eschatology, and what pertains to that of the states of possible things. Hence the people restricted themselves in refuting that the subject of kalām is the Essence of God alone or with the essence of possible things from the aspect of dependence, on the grounds that if it were so, then its proof would not be among the issues of kalām, because the subject of a science is not explained in it, but in a higher science, until it reaches that whose subject is evident in existence, like the existent. That is because the reality of a science is to prove the essential attributes of a thing according to the meaning of the composite quiddity (al-haythiyya al-murakkaba), and it is not hidden that it is after the simple quiddity (al-haythiyya al-basīṭa), for that whose existence is not known cannot have anything attributed to it. But there is no dispute that proving the Necessary Being in the sense of establishing the proof for His existence is among the highest issues of kalām. Then, His being the principle of possible things by choice or necessity, without an intermediary in all or with an intermediary in some, is another discussion. And the claim that proving Him is only among the issues of metaphysics (al-ilāhī) and not of kalām is clearly corrupt; otherwise, it would not be one of the Islamic sciences, indeed it is the chief of them, their head, the foundation of the legal principles, and their basis. And some answered that it is permissible here to prove the subject in the science for two reasons: First: that existence is among His essential attributes because He is the Necessary Existent, unlike other sciences, for existence only attaches to their subjects from an external cause. And this was the meaning of the one who said:
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer regarding whether proving the existence of the Creator (al-Ṣāni‘) belongs to the issues (maṭālib) of kalam. The objection states that the subject of a science is not proven within that science but in a higher science; since the existence of God is the subject of kalam, proving it cannot be an issue of kalam. Taftazani responds first by noting that later scholars mixed metaphysical discussions into kalam for beneficial purposes, but the core of kalam is limited to discussions of the Essence, attributes, prophethood, imamate, and eschatology, along with states of possible things related to these. The refuters argue that if the subject were God alone or with possible things, then proving God would not be a kalam issue. But Taftazani insists that it is a common issue that proving the Necessary Being is among the highest issues of kalam. He then rejects the claim that it belongs only to metaphysics (al-ilāhī), because kalam is the chief Islamic science. Finally, he presents an answer from some scholars: it is permissible to prove the subject within kalam for two reasons. The first reason is that existence is an essential attribute (ʿaraḍ dhātī) of the Necessary Being, whereas in other sciences existence is accidental to the subject. The text ends mid-sentence at 'وكان هذا مراد من قال:' suggesting the second reason or conclusion is missing in this excerpt.

Misconception: Proving the subject of a science within that science is always invalid.
Correction: It is permissible in kalām because existence is an essential attribute of the Necessary Being, unlike other sciences.

?
If existence is an essential attribute of God, then He is the source of all existence. Are there areas of your life where you seek security or identity from created things rather than from Him? How can you realign your trust today?

The First Response: Existence as an Essential Accident
First response: existence as essential accident with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first response to the objection that the subject (God) cannot be proven within kalam, and how the concept of existence as an essential accident is used to justify it.

Core Idea
Existence is an essential accident of the Necessary Being, allowing its proof within kalam.

The objection that kalam cannot prove its own subject is met with a sophisticated response: existence is an essential accident of the Necessary Being, so proving existence falls within the scope of kalam. This passage presents the response, the objection from those who hold different views on existence, and the final answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the flow: a response, then an objection, then an answer.
The passage uses kalam technical terms like 'essential accident' (عرض ذاتي) and 'acquired proposition' (قضية كسبية).
2
Identify the core argument
The first response claims that existence is an essential accident of the Necessary Being. Why does this matter? Because it means that proving existence is part of investigating the states of the subject.
Essential accidents are properties that necessarily belong to a thing.
3
Understand the objection
The objection states that this doesn't work for two views: existence identical to quiddity, or existence as a common additional attribute. The objection argues that essential accidents must be specific.
If existence is common, how can it be an essential accident of a specific being?
4
Analyze the answer
The answer is that regardless of the view, if there is a proposition whose predicate is existence by necessity, the proof stands; otherwise the objection collapses.
This shows the argument does not depend on a particular ontology of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجاب بعضهم بأنه جاز ههنا إثبات الموضوع في العلم لوجهتين : الأول : أن الوجود من أعراضه الذاتية لكونه واجب الوجود بخلاف سائر العلوم، فإن الوجود إنما يلحق موضوعاتها لأمر مباين وكان هذا مراد من قال : غير موضوع العلم إنما لا يبين فيه إذ كان البحث فيه عن الأحوال التي الوجود، وإلا فهذه التفرقة مما لا يشهد بها عقل ولا نقل بل ليس لها كثير معنى . فإن قيل : هذا لا يصح على رأي من يجعل الوجود نفس الماهية وهو ظاهر، ولا على رأي من يجعله زائداً مشتركاً، لأن العرض الذاتي يكون مختصاً. قلنا سواء كان ذاته نفس الوجود أو غيره، فإما أن يكون هناك قضية كسبية محمولها الموجود في الخارج بطريق الوجوب، فيتم الجواب أو لا فيسقط أصل الاعتراض.
And some answered that it is permissible here to prove the subject in the science for two aspects: First, that existence is among its essential accidents because it is the Necessary Existent, contrary to other sciences, for existence only attaches to their subjects for a different reason. This was the meaning of the one who said: 'The subject of a science is not established within it, since the investigation therein is about the states that are existence, otherwise this distinction is not attested to by reason or tradition, nor does it have much meaning.' Then it is said: This is not valid according to the view of one who makes existence identical to quiddity, which is apparent, nor according to the view of one who makes it additional and common, because the essential accident is specific. We said: Whether His essence is existence itself or other, either there is an acquired proposition whose predicate is the existent in the external world by way of necessity, then the answer is complete, or not, then the basis of the objection collapses.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the first response to the objection that proving God's existence is not within kalam because the subject is not proven within the science. The response argues that existence is an essential accident (عرض ذاتي) of the Necessary Being, so it is proper to prove it within kalam. The author then gives an objection from those who hold that existence is either identical to quiddity or a common additional attribute, and the essential accident must be specific. The answer is that regardless, if there is a proposition whose predicate is existence by necessity, the proof stands; otherwise the objection collapses.

Correction: The answer shows that the proof does not depend on a specific view of existence; it works as long as there is a proposition asserting existence by necessity. Thus the objection fails.

Second Response and Its Three Criticisms
Second response with three objections refuting it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second response to the problem of establishing the subject of kalam and the three objections that refute it.

Core Idea
The second response fails because essential accidents need no outside science, and its own criteria make it self-refuting.

This passage presents a key debate: can the subject of kalam (God's existence) be established within kalam itself? One argument says no higher science exists, so it must be done within kalam. But the author raises three sharp objections, showing why this response is weak. Let's examine the argument and its flaws step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the response
Read the initial statement of the second response. Notice that it argues that because there is no higher revealed science, the subject must be established within kalam.
This is the position being critiqued.
2
Examine the first objection
The first objection states that it is not a condition of an essential accident that it be unknown to others. Examples like health and sickness for man show that essential accidents can be known in other sciences.
This undermines the need for a higher science.
3
Examine the second objection
The second objection argues that if the principle were applied strictly, then proving the existence of any possible being would not be an issue in any science, which is false.
This shows the principle leads to absurd consequences.
4
Examine the third objection
The third objection points out that the statement 'the subject is not proven within the science' becomes meaningless, because existence is an essential accident of the subject and is proven within the science.
This reveals the response is self-defeating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لا علم شرعي فوقه يبين فيه موضوعه، فلا بد من بيان فيه، وفيه نظر . وأما أولاً : فلأنه ليس من شرط العرض الذاتي أن لا يكون معلوماً للغير، بل أن لا يكون لحوقه للشيء بتوسط لحوقه لأمر خارج غير مساو للاتفاق على كون الصحة والمرض عرضاً ذاتياً للإنسان والحركة والسكون للجسم، والاستقامة والانحناء للخط إلى غير ذلك . وأما ثانياً : فلأنه يلزم أن لا يكون ببيان وجود شيء من الممكنات مسألة في شيء من العلوم فلا يصح أن موضوع العلم إنما يبين وجوده في علم أعلى . وأما ثالثاً : فلأن قولهم موضوع العلم لا يبين فيه بعد تقدير أنه لا يثبت في العلم غير الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع يكون لغواً من الكلام، لأن وجوده عرض ذاتي بين فيه، وما لا يبين ليس بعرض ذاتي .
Second, that there is no revealed science above it that establishes its subject, so it is necessary to establish it within it. But there is consideration in this. As for first, because it is not a condition of the essential accident that it not be known to others, but rather that its adherence to the thing not be by mediation of adherence to an external matter that is not equal, as agreed upon that health and sickness are essential accidents for man, and motion and rest for body, and straightness and curvature for line, and so on. As for second, because it would necessitate that proving the existence of any possible being would not be an issue in any science, so it would not be valid that the subject of a science is established in a higher science. As for third, because their statement 'the subject of a science is not established within it' after assuming that nothing is established in the science other than the essential accidents of the subject becomes meaningless speech, because its existence is an essential accident established therein, and what is not established is not an essential accident.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the second response: there is no revealed science higher than kalam that proves its subject (God's existence), so it must be proven within kalam. The author then raises three objections. First, it is not a condition of an essential accident that it be unknown to others; examples include health and sickness for man. Second, if the principle were applied strictly, then proving the existence of any possible being would not be an issue in any science, which is false. Third, the very statement that the subject is not proven within the science becomes meaningless, because existence is an essential accident proven within, and what is not proven is not an essential accident. These objections show the weakness of the second response.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second response is a valid defense of kalam's self-sufficiency.
Correction: The three objections show it is flawed: essential accidents can be known elsewhere, the principle leads to absurdities, and it becomes self-refuting.

Checkpoint

B) Essential accidents can be known to others.

D) Proving existence of possible beings is not part of any science.

Key Takeaway
The second response fails because its own criteria make it self-refuting: the subject's existence is an essential accident proven within the science.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage uses examples like health and sickness for man to show essential accidents are not exclusive. In your life, have you ever assumed a truth or practice belongs only to one area (like faith vs. reason) when actually it is shared? How does that affect your understanding of knowledge?
?
The third objection reveals that the very statement 'the subject is not proven within' becomes meaningless if existence is an essential accident. When you say 'I don't need to prove this belief—it's just foundational,' could that belief actually be an essential accident that you must examine? Will you dare to test it?
