Five Counter-Arguments to the Second Objection
The author presents five logical counter-arguments to the second objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five counter-arguments that the author uses to refute the claim that kalam must explain its subject matter within itself.

Core Idea
The author provides five reasons why the subject of kalam need not be explained in a higher science.

In this passage, the author addresses the second objection to the need for a higher science to explain the subject matter of kalam. He systematically presents five counter-arguments, each challenging a different aspect of the objection. As you read, pay attention to how he uses logical reasoning and examples to defend the autonomy of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the author's response
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author begins with the objection, then lists five counter-arguments using 'أما أولاً' etc.
2
Understand the first counter-argument
Focus on the first point: the condition for an essential accident is not unknownness but non-mediation. Consider the examples of health and sickness for man.
This refutes the idea that the subject must be explained in a higher science.
3
Analyze the second counter-argument
The second argues that if the objection were valid, no existence of any possible would be a problem in any science, which is false.
4
Review the remaining three counter-arguments
Go through the third, fourth, and fifth points. The third shows the claim becomes nonsense, the fourth challenges the universality of subject-principles-problems, and the fifth discusses the hierarchy of sciences.
5
Synthesize the overall rebuttal
Consider how all five points together undermine the objection. The author concludes that explaining a religious subject does not require the science to be religious.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : أنه لا علم شرعي فوقه يبين فيه موضوعه، فلا بد من بيان فيه، وفيه نظر . أما أولاً : فلأنه ليس من شرط العرض الذاتي أن لا يكون معلوماً للغير، بل أن لا يكون لحوقه للشيء بتوسط لحوقه لأمر خارج غير مساو للاتفاق على كون الصحة والمرض عرضاً ذاتياً للإنسان والحركة والسكون للجسم، والاستقامة والانحناء للخط إلى غير ذلك . وأما ثانياً : فلأنه يلزم أن لا يكون ببيان وجود شيء من الممكنات مسألة في شيء من العلوم فلا يصح أن موضوع العلم إنما يبين وجوده في علم أعلى . وأما ثالثاً : فلأن قولهم موضوع العلم لا يبين فيه بعد تقدير أنه لا يثبت في العلم غير الأعراض الذاتية للموضوع يكون لغواً من الكلام، لأن وجوده عرض ذاتي بين فيه، وما لا يبين ليس بعرض ذاتي . وأما رابعاً : فلأنه لا يبقى قولهم لكل علم موضوع ومبادىء ومسائل على عمومه، لأن معناه التصديق بآنية الموضوع وهلية البسيطة، وقد صار في علم الكلام من جملة المسائل. وأما خامساً : فلأن تصاعد العلوم، إنما هو بتصاعد الموضوعات فلا معنى لكون علم أعلى من آخر سوى أن موضوعه أعم، فينبغي أن يؤخذ موضوع علم الكلام الموجود أو المعلوم، وإلا فالإلهي أعلى منه رتبة، وإن كان هو أشرف من جهة، وقد عرفت أن ما يبين فيه موضوع علم شرعي أو مبادئه لا يلزم أن يكون علماً شرعياً، بل يكفي كونه تعينياً وعلى وفق الشرع.
The second: That there is no religious science above it that explains its subject matter, so it must be explained in it, and it is open to consideration. As for first: Because it is not a condition of the essential accident that it be unknown to others, but rather that its inherence in the thing not be through the mediation of its inherence in an external thing that is not equal, according to the consensus that health and sickness are essential accidents of man, and motion and rest of body, and straightness and curvature of line, etc. As for second: Because it would entail that the explanation of the existence of any possible thing would not be a question in any of the sciences, so it is not valid that the subject matter of a science is only explained in a higher science. As for third: Because their statement that the subject of a science is not explained in it, after supposing that only essential accidents of the subject are established in the science, becomes nonsense, because existence is an essential accident that is explained in it, and what is not explained is not an essential accident. As for fourth: Because their statement that every science has a subject, principles, and problems does not remain universal, because its meaning is the affirmation of the thatness of the subject and the simple state, and that has become in the science of kalam among the problems. As for fifth: Because the hierarchy of sciences is only by the hierarchy of subjects, so there is no meaning to one science being higher than another except that its subject is more general. So the subject of kalam should be taken as existence or the known. Otherwise, metaphysics is higher than it in rank, even if it is nobler in some respect. And you have known that what is explained in it of a subject of a religious science or its principles does not necessitate that it be a religious science; rather it suffices that it be determinative and in accordance with the law.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and five counter-arguments. The objection states that since there is no higher religious science that explains the subject of kalam, it must be explained within kalam itself. The author finds this objection problematic and offers five points. First, the condition for an essential accident is not that it be unknown, but that it inheres without mediation of an external thing, as seen in examples like health for man. Second, if the objection were valid, no existence of any possible would be a question in any science, contradicting the fact that existence can be a problem in a higher science. Third, the claim that the subject is not explained becomes meaningless if only essential accidents are established, since existence is such an accident. Fourth, the universal statement that every science has a subject, principles, and problems loses its universality, because affirming the thatness of the subject becomes a problem in kalam. Fifth, the hierarchy of sciences is by subject generality, so kalam's subject should be existence or the known, otherwise metaphysics would be higher in rank. Yet, explaining a religious subject does not require that science to be religious; it suffices that it be determinative and lawful.

How a Higher Science Explains the Specific Subject
The author answers an objection about how a higher science explains the specific subject of a lower science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a higher science, through division of its general subject, can establish the existence of the specific subject of a lower science without necessitating it.

Core Idea
A higher science explains the specific by dividing the general, not by entailment.

This passage addresses a crucial methodological question in the classification of sciences: how does a higher science like metaphysics provide the existence of subjects for lower sciences? The objection claims a circularity, but the answer shows a logical process of division. Understanding this clarifies the relationship between sciences and their subjects.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the opening objection to understand the problem: if the specific existence is not explained in its own science because it is self-evident or explained in a higher science, then the higher science's explanation would not be part of the composite state, and the subject would not be assumed to exist.
Notice the objection's structure: 'If it is said... then the problem arises.'
2
Understand the answer
The answer clarifies that the higher science's subject is more general. Its existence does not entail the specific. Instead, the higher science explains the specific by showing the division of the general into it and other parts.
The key term 'هلية مركبة' (composite state) is central.
3
Study the example
The author gives an example from metaphysics (some existents are bodies) and physics (some bodies are spheres). This illustrates the division process.
4
Reflect on the hinted critique
The author hints that the discerning reader may detect a flaw in some related positions. Consider what that might be.
This connects to the broader methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فقد آل الكلام إلى أن الوجود المخصص لموضوع الصناعة، وإن كان من أغراضه الذاتية لا يبين فيها لكون نظرها مقصوراً على بيان هليته المركبة، بل ولأنه مسلماً في نظرها لكونه بيناً أو مبيناً في صناعة أعلى، وحينئذ يتوجه الإشكال بأن بيانه هناك لا يكون من الهلية المركبة وموضوع هذا العرض الذاتي لا يكون مما هو مسلم الوجود . قلنا : موضوع الصناعة الأعلى أعم، ووجوده لا يستلزم وجود الأخص فيبين فيها وجود الأخص بأن يبين انقسام الأعم إليه وإلى غيره، وأنه يوجد له هذا القسم، ويكون ذلك عائداً إلى الهلية المركبة للأعم، مثلاً يبين في الإلهي أن بعض الموجود جسم، فيبين وجود الجسم، وفي الطبيعي أن بعض الجسم كرة، فيبين وجود الكرة . سبق . وعلى هذا القياس ربما يتنبه الفطن من هذا الكلام لنكتة قادحة في بعض ما
If it is said: The discussion has led to the point that the specific existence for the subject of the craft, even if it is among its essential purposes, is not explained in it because its view is confined to explaining its composite state, and also because it is considered in its view as self-evident or explained in a higher craft. Then the problem arises that its explanation there is not from the composite state, and the subject of this essential accident is not something whose existence is assumed. We say: The subject of the higher craft is more general, and its existence does not necessitate the existence of the more specific. So it explains the existence of the more specific by showing the division of the general into it and other than it, and that this division exists for it, and that goes back to the composite state of the general. For example, in metaphysics it is shown that some existents are bodies, so it explains the existence of body; and in physics that some bodies are spheres, so it explains the existence of the sphere. Previously. And by this analogy, perhaps the intelligent person may notice from this speech a point that invalidates some of what...
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning how a higher science explains the specific subject of a lower science. The objection argues that if the specific existence for a craft's subject is not explained within that craft because its view is limited to the composite state, and it is considered given or explained in a higher craft, then a problem arises: the explanation in the higher craft would not be part of the composite state, and the subject of the essential accident would not be assumed to exist. The answer clarifies that the subject of the higher craft is more general. Its existence does not entail the existence of the more specific. Instead, the higher craft explains the existence of the more specific by showing the division of the general into it and other parts, and that this division actually occurs. This explanation returns to the composite state of the general. The author gives an example: metaphysics shows that some existents are bodies, thus establishing the existence of body; then physics shows that some bodies are spheres, establishing the existence of the sphere. The word 'sabaqa' (previously) indicates this argument has been referenced before, and the author hints that the discerning reader may detect a flaw in some related positions.

Key Terms
هلية مركبة
صناعة أعلى
أعم
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The general subject of a higher science automatically necessitates the specific existence for a lower science.
Correction: The author clarifies that the general only explains the specific through division, not automatic entailment.

Checkpoint
What example does the author give to illustrate the explanation of a specific subject in a higher science?
A) In metaphysics, some existents are bodies; in physics, some bodies are spheres
B) In physics, some bodies are spheres; in metaphysics, some spheres are bodies
C) Only that in metaphysics, some existents are bodies
D) Only that in physics, some bodies are spheres

Key Takeaway
A higher science explains the existence of a lower science's subject by dividing its own general subject, not by assuming it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a subject, do you intentionally connect it to broader principles from higher sciences, or do you treat it as isolated? How might this habit be limiting your understanding?
?
Have you ever assumed that knowing a general rule automatically gives you mastery over its specific applications? Where has that assumption led you to miss important details or make mistakes?
?
This lesson shows that higher sciences explain specifics by division, not entailment. Reflect on a time when you wrongly concluded that a general principle guaranteed a specific outcome. What did you overlook, and how can you guard against that in the future?

Objection to the Division of Knowledge and Its Answers
Objection with two answers and clarification of tasawwur.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that tasawwur and tasdiq cannot be divided due to necessity and the two responses that resolve it.

Core Idea
Existential necessity does not prevent conceptual contrast.

Scholars objected that dividing knowledge into conception and assent is flawed because one requires the other. This passage presents the objection and two key responses, clarifying that the division stands on conceptual grounds and that the correct conception for assent is 'without condition of judgment.'

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
The passage opens by stating that some rejected the division due to necessity (luzum): there is no assent without conception. Read carefully to see the initial objection.
Notice that the objection is based on existential dependence.
2
Examine the first answer
The first response redefines the division by introducing 'simple conception' (tasawwur sādhij) as conception conditioned by absence of judgment. See how this changes the categories.
3
Study the second answer
The second response argues that necessity in existence does not preclude contrast in concept, using the example of pair and odd. Understand why this preserves the division.
This is the more sophisticated answer.
4
Grasp the correct understanding of tasawwur
The passage concludes that the tasawwur relevant to tasdiq is the one 'without condition of judgment' (lā bi-sharṭ al-ḥukm), not the one conditioned by absence. See the distinction.

ثم الصدق
وأجاب آخرون بأن اللزوم بحسب الوجود لا ينافي التقابل بحسب كما بين الزوج والفرد والحصر في التصور المقيد بعدم الحكم، وفي التصديق ليس بتام لخروج تصور الطرفين وبالجملة فكلام القوم صريح في أن التصور المعتبر في التصديق هو التصور المقابل له، وهو التصور لا بشرط الحكم أعني الذي لم يعتبر فيه الحكم لا الذي اعتبر فيه عدم الحكم، وصرح الإمام (۱) والكاتبي (۲) بأن هذا هو المراد بالتصور الساذج والتصور فقط، وحاصل التقسيم أن العلم إما أن يعتبر فيه الحكم وهو التصديق، أو لا وهو التصور.
ومعناه : أن التصديق هو الحكم مع ما يتعلق به من التصورات، على ما هو صريح كلام الإمام ، لا الإدراك المقيد بالحكم على ما سبق إلى الفهم من عبارته، حيث يقول : إنه الإدراك المقارن للحكم ، أو الإدراك الذي يلحقه الحكم كيف وأنه يذكر ذلك في معرض الاستدلال على كون التصور جزءاً منه، ثم إنه كثيراً ما يصرخ بأنه عبارة عن نفس الحكم، ويجعل الحكم تارة من قبيل الأفعال، وتارة ماهية (٣) مسماة بالكلام النفسي ليست من جنس الاعتقاد ولا الإرادة .
والجمهور على أنه نفس الحكم، وأنه نوع من العلم متميز عن التصور بحقيقته لا يتعلق إلا بالنسبة بخلاف التصور حيث يتعلق بها وبغيرها . ألا ترى أنك إذا شككت في حدوث العالم فقد تصورت العالم والحادث والنسبة بينهما من غير حكم وتصديق، ثم إذا أقيم البرهان فقد علمت النسبة نوعاً آخر من

(۱) الإمام هو فخر الدين الرازي . راجع ترجمة له فيما تقدم.
(۲) الكاتبي : علي بن عمر بن علي الكاتبي القزويني نجم الدين ويقال له وبران، حكيم منطقي، من تلاميذه نصير الدين الطوسي له تصانيف منها الشمسية رسالة في قواعد المنطق، وحكمة العين في المنطق والطبيعي والرياضي ، والمفصل شرح المحصل لفخر الدين الرازي في الكلام، جامع الدقائق في كشف الحقائق . منطق . توفي سنة ٦٧٥هـ.
راجع فوات الوفيات : ٦٦٢ .
(۳) سقط من (ب) لفظ (ماهية).
And some rejected it because of the necessity between them, since there is no assent without conception, rather they mentioned that there is no conception in reality without certainty of assent, and the discussion is about conception by name, so they turned to the division into simple conception, i.e., conditioned by absence of judgment, and into assent.
Then truth (al-sidq)
And others answered that necessity in existence does not preclude contrast in concept, as between pair and odd, and the restriction to conception conditioned by absence of judgment is not perfect because it excludes conception of the two terms. In sum, the statements of the people are explicit that the conception considered in assent is the conception opposite to it, which is conception without the condition of judgment, i.e., that in which judgment is not considered, not that in which absence of judgment is considered. And the Imam and al-Katibi explicitly stated that this is meant by simple conception and by conception alone. The upshot of the division is that knowledge is either considered with judgment, which is assent, or not, which is conception.
And its meaning: that assent is the judgment along with the conceptions related to it, according to the explicit statement of the Imam, not perception conditioned by judgment as might be understood from his expression, where he says: it is perception accompanied by judgment, or perception to which judgment attaches. How so? He mentions that in the context of arguing that conception is part of it. Then he often declares that it is the very judgment, and he considers judgment sometimes as an act, sometimes as an essence called inner speech, not of the genus of belief or will. The majority holds that it is the very judgment, and that it is a type of knowledge distinct from conception in its reality, pertaining only to relation, unlike conception which pertains to it and to other things. Do you not see that if you doubt the occurrence of the world, you have conceived the world, occurrence, and the relation between them without judgment and assent. Then when proof is established, you know the relation as another type of [knowledge].

(1) The Imam is Fakhr al-Din al-Razi. See his biography earlier.
(2) Al-Katibi: Ali ibn Umar ibn Ali al-Katibi al-Qazwini, Najm al-Din, also called Wabran, a logical philosopher, among his students Nasir al-Din al-Tusi. He has works including al-Shamsiyya on logic, Hikmat al-Ayn on logic, natural, and mathematical sciences, and al-Mufassal Sharh al-Muhassal of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi on kalam, Jami' al-Daqa'iq fi Kashf al-Haqa'iq on logic. Died 675 AH. See Fawat al-Wafayat: 662.
(3) In version (b), the word 'essence' (mahiyya) is missing.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection against the division of knowledge into tasawwur and tasdiq, then explains the nature of tasdiq itself. The objection states that the division is flawed because tasawwur is necessary for tasdiq, so they cannot be mutually exclusive categories. The first response is that some scholars simply redefined the division, distinguishing between ‘simple conception’ (tasawwur sādhij) — which is conditioned by the absence of judgment — and tasdiq. The second response, provided by ‘others,’ argues that existential necessity (luzūm wujūdī) does not prevent conceptual contrast (taqābul bi-ḥasab), just as ‘pair’ and ‘odd’ are opposite in concept despite one implying the other in certain contexts. They further note that restricting the term ‘conception’ to the conditioned type is imperfect because it excludes the conception of the two terms in a judgment. The correct understanding, explicitly endorsed by Imam al-Razi and al-Katibi, is that the tasawwur relevant to tasdiq is the one ‘without the condition of judgment’ (lā bi-sharṭ al-ḥukm) — meaning judgment is simply not considered in it, not that absence of judgment is stipulated. Hence the upshot: knowledge either involves judgment (tasdiq) or does not (tasawwur). The passage then discusses the meaning of tasdiq itself. According to Imam al-Razi, tasdiq is the judgment (ḥukm) together with the conceptions it entails, not perception conditioned by judgment. He sometimes calls it the very judgment, considering judgment as an act or as an inner speech (kalām nafsī) distinct from belief and will. The majority (jumhūr) holds that tasdiq is the judgment itself — a type of knowledge distinct in reality from tasawwur, applying only to relations (nusba), whereas tasawwur can apply to relations as well as to terms. The example illustrates: when you doubt the world’s creation, you have tasawwur (conceptions of world, creation, and the relation without judgment); after proof (burhān), you attain tasdiq — a different kind of knowledge. The final sentence is cut off at the page break, but the argument is otherwise complete.

تصور ساذج
لا بشرط الحكم
المشروط بعدم الحكم
التقابل بحسب المفهوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The division between tasawwur and tasdiq is invalid because they are necessarily connected.
Correction: The passage explains that existential necessity does not preclude conceptual contrast; the two are distinct in definition despite dependence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct understanding of tasawwur relevant to tasdiq?
A) Tasawwur conditioned by absence of judgment.
B) Tasawwur without the condition of judgment (lā bi-sharṭ al-ḥukm).
C) Tasawwur as the opposite of tasdiq.
D) Tasawwur as necessary for tasdiq.

Key Takeaway
The division of knowledge into conception and assent is valid because they are conceptually distinct even though existentially connected.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever insisted that two things must be the same because one cannot exist without the other? Where in your own life or belief have you confused existential connection with conceptual identity? This lesson forces you to examine: Are you ready to accept that Allah's attributes are conceptually distinct even though they are all present in His essence? What practical difference does that make in how you worship Him?
?
When you approach knowledge of Islam, do you rush to assent (tasdiq) before properly forming a conception (tasawwur)? How has this lesson exposed a dangerous shortcut in your own learning? Will you commit to pausing to ensure you have genuinely conceived of a matter before you affirm it as true?
?
The pair-odd analogy shows that things can be opposite in concept yet existentially linked. Is there a relationship in your life—between your heart and actions, or between your knowledge of good and your doing good—where you have misapplied this principle? How can you now distinguish the conceptual from the existential to bring more clarity to your spiritual struggle?

The Nature of Tasdiq: Judgment and Inner Speech
Discussion of the definition of tasdiq as judgment with conceptions, inner speech, and majority view with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of tasdiq as judgment with its related conceptions, and the majority view that it is a distinct type of knowledge called inner speech.

Core Idea
Tasdiq is judgment itself, distinct from conception and pertains to relations.

After clarifying the division, the passage delves into the definition of tasdiq. Imam al-Razi and the majority offer nuanced views: tasdiq is the judgment along with its conceptions, yet it is itself a type of knowledge distinct from conception. This lesson explores those definitions and the example of doubt and proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of tasdiq according to Imam al-Razi
The passage states that tasdiq is the judgment (ḥukm) together with the conceptions related to it, not perception conditioned by judgment. Pay attention to the nuance.
Contrast this with a simpler definition that might confuse.
2
Understand the two interpretations of judgment
Imam al-Razi sometimes considers judgment as an act, sometimes as an essence called inner speech (kalām nafsī), distinct from belief and will. Note that this is a metaphysical claim.
Inner speech is a key concept in kalam.
3
Grasp the majority view
The majority (jumhūr) holds that tasdiq is the very judgment, a type of knowledge distinct from tasawwur, applying only to relations.
4
Analyze the example of doubt and proof
The passage illustrates with the example of doubting the world's creation: you have tasawwur of terms without judgment; after proof, you have tasdiq as a different kind of knowledge of the relation.
Note that the example ends abruptly due to page break.

?

?

?

How the Unknown Can Be Sought: The Principle of Partial Knowledge
Al-Taftazani addresses an objection regarding the object of tasdiq and tasawwur and explains how the unknown can be sought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic theologians resolved the paradox of seeking knowledge of an unknown thing through the key distinction that it is known in one respect (ma'lum min wajh).

Core Idea
The unknown can be sought because it is known in one respect, not absolutely unknown.

In this passage, al-Taftazani tackles a fundamental challenge to acquiring new conceptual knowledge: how can we direct our minds to something we have never known? The objection claims it is impossible, but the answer introduces a crucial distinction—the object is known from one angle, allowing us to pursue its unknown dimensions. This lesson unpacks that solution and its implications for kalam methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the objection-answer structure and the key terms.
The passage references multiple works; focus on the central argument.
2
Identify the key distinction
Find where the author says the object is 'known in one respect' (ma'lum min wajh). Understand how this resolves the impossibility.
The known aspect removes absolute unknownness.
3
Examine the examples
The author uses the example of knowing we have 'something with life and perceptions' and then seeking its essence or accidents. See how this illustrates partial knowledge leading to further inquiry.
4
Analyze the references to other works
Consider the references to al-Mawāqif and Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal. These support and refine the argument, showing scholarly dialogue.
The critique of Talkhīṣ highlights the difference between known and unknown aspects.
5
Reflect on the significance
Think about why this principle is foundational: it allows the kalam project of seeking knowledge of God and creation despite initial ignorance.
This connects to the broader methodology of acquiring conceptualization (tasawwur).

وحاصله أن متعلق التصديق يجوز أن يتعلق به قبل التصديق علم بخلاف متعلق التصور . وأجيب بأن تختار أنه معلوم من وجه ولا نسلم امتناع التوجه حينئذ إلى وجهه المجهول، وإنما يمتنع لو لم يكن الوجه المعلوم من وجوهه واعتباراته بحيث يخرجه عن كونه مجهولاً مطلقاً، وذلك كما إذا علمنا أن لنا شيئاً به الحياة والإدراكات فتطلبه بحقيقته أو بعوارضه المميزة له عن جميع ما عداه، على ما هو المستفاد من الحد أو الرسم. فالمجهول المطلوب لا ينحصر في الحقيقة ولا في العارض وما ذكر في المواقف من أن المجهول هو الذات والمعلوم بعض الاعتبارات تحقيق لما هو الأهم، أعني إمكان اكتساب التصور بحسب الحقيقة وتنبيه على أن مجهولية الذات لازمة فيما يطلب تصوره حتى لو على الشيء ء بحقيقته، وقصد اكتساب بعض العوارض له كان ذلك بالدليل لا التعريف، ولو قصد اكتساب العارض نفسه كان هو مجهولاً بحقيقته، وما ذكر في تلخيص المحصل من أن كلا من الوجه المجهول والمعلوم حاصل لأمر ثالث هو المطلوب؛ إلزام للإمام بما اعترف به من أن المعلوم إجمالاً : معلوم من وجه . مجهول من وجه) والوجهان متغايران أحدهما معلوم لا إجمال فيه، والآخر ليس بمعلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء ظن أن هناك علماً إجمالياً. وإلا فقد ذكر هو في نقد تنزيل الأفكار : أن المطلوب المجهول هو حقيقة الماهية المعلومة من جهة بعض عوارضها
Its conclusion: the object of assent may be known before assent, unlike the object of conceptualization. It was answered: we choose that it is known in one respect, and we do not grant the impossibility of then directing [attention] to its unknown aspect; rather it is impossible only if the known aspect of its aspects and considerations does not remove it from being absolutely unknown. That is like when we know that we have something through which life and perceptions exist, and we seek it by its essence or by its distinguishing accidents from all other things, as is understood from definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm). The sought unknown is not confined to the essence nor to the accident. What was mentioned in al-Mawāqif—that the unknown is the essence and the known is some considerations—is a verification of what is most important, namely the possibility of acquiring conceptualization with respect to the essence, and a reminder that the essence's unknownness is necessary in what one seeks to conceptualize, such that even if one seeks the thing by its essence and intends to acquire some of its accidents, that would be by proof (dalīl) not definition (taʻrīf). And if one intends to acquire the accident itself, it is unknown in its essence. And what was mentioned in Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal—that each of the unknown and known aspects is realized for a third thing which is sought—is an imposition upon the Imām (al-Rāzī) because he admitted that what is known in summary is known in one respect and unknown in one respect. The two aspects are different: one is known without any summary, and the other is not known at all. But when they combine in something, one thinks there is summary knowledge. Otherwise, he himself mentioned in Naqd Tanzīl al-Afkār: the sought unknown is the essence of the quiddity (māhiyya) known through some of its accidents.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection concerning the possibility of seeking conceptualization of an unknown thing. The author (al-Taftazānī) begins by stating a conclusion: the object of assent (taṣdīq) can be known before the assent occurs, unlike the object of conceptualization (taṣawwur). An objection arises: how can we direct our attention to something completely unknown? The answer is that the object is known in one respect (min wajh) and unknown in another; thus it is not absolutely unknown. The known aspect—such as knowing that we have something with life and perceptions—allows us to seek its essence or distinguishing accidents, as per definition (ḥadd) or description (rasm). The author then clarifies that the sought unknown is not limited to the essence or accidents alone. He references al-Mawāqif, which states that the unknown is the essence (dhāt) and the known is some considerations (iʻtibārāt), thereby affirming the possibility of acquiring conceptualization of the essence. If one seeks the essence itself and aims to learn its accidents, that is done through proof (dalīl), not definition. If one seeks an accident, that accident itself is unknown in its essence. The passage then critiques a view from Talkhīṣ al-Muḥaṣṣal, which posits a third thing that is sought, arguing that it forces an admission from Imām al-Rāzī that summary knowledge (al-maʻlūm ijimālan) is known in one respect and unknown in another. The two aspects are distinct: one is completely clear, the other completely unknown, but their combination gives an illusion of summary knowledge. Finally, from Naqd Tanzīl al-Afkār, it is stated that the sought unknown is the essence of the quiddity (māhiyya) known through some of its accidents.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that seeking knowledge of an unknown thing is impossible unless the thing is completely known beforehand.
Correction: The passage clarifies that partial knowledge (known in one respect) is sufficient to initiate inquiry; the unknown aspect is pursued based on what is known.

Checkpoint
According to the answer presented, what condition makes it possible to seek an unknown thing?
A) The thing must be completely unknown.
B) The thing must be known in one aspect.
C) The thing must be known through a definition.
D) The thing must be the object of assent.

Key Takeaway
The core principle for seeking the unknown is that the object must be known in at least one respect, preventing absolute unknownness and allowing further inquiry.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own journey to know Allah, you have some knowledge—His names, attributes, creation. But have you stopped there, satisfied with that partial knowledge, without seeking to know Him more deeply through reflection on His signs? What keeps you from directing your attention to the unknown aspects of His majesty?
?
Consider a fault in yourself that you acknowledge vaguely—like 'I have a bad temper.' That is knowing in one respect. Are you truly seeking to know its root, its triggers, its full reality? Or do you avoid the effort because it feels like the unknown is impossible to grasp? How will you actively seek it now?
?
This lesson teaches that the unknown is sought because it is known in one respect. What is the one respect in which you know your own soul? And what is the unknown essence of your soul that you must pursue through self-accounting (muhasaba) and struggle? Will you settle for a superficial knowledge of yourself, or will you press on to the depths?

Method of Learning
intermediate
8 min
Impossibility of Defining Essence by Itself or External Part
Al-Taftazani proves that the definer of an essence cannot be the essence itself or an external part, and addresses three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the definer of an essence cannot be the essence itself, its parts, or something external, and why definition by the external leads to circularity and infinite regress.

Core Idea
Defining an essence by itself is impossible; defining by external leads to circularity.

In this passage, al-Taftazani continues his logical analysis of definition. Having established preliminaries, he now argues that the definer of a quiddity cannot be the quiddity itself, nor any part of it, nor something external. He shows that each option leads to absurdities: circularity or infinite regress. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. The argument is dense; do not worry about understanding everything immediately.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the first of three objections is just introduced.
2
Identify the three possibilities for the definer
The definer of a quiddity must be either the quiddity itself, all its parts (which is the same), some parts, or something external. Notice how each is eliminated.
The passage focuses on the part leading to the external.
3
Understand why definition by the external fails
Definition by the external requires prior knowledge of the quiddity (circularity) and knowledge of all other distinct things (impossible). This is a key logical point.
The author also critiques a statement from al-Mawāqif for being imprecise.
4
Reflect on the structure of the argument
Notice how the argument uses hypothetical cases and eliminates each to show that only impossible options remain. This is a classic mutakallimūn method.
The three objections mentioned will be explored in later lessons.

وأما الثاني : فلأن الكاسب - أعني المعرف للماهية يمتنع أن يكون نفسها لامتناع كون الشيء أجلى وأسرع معرفة من نفسه بل يكون إما جميع أجزائها وهو نفسها، فيعود المحذور وإما بعضها، أو خارجاً عنها. ويندرج فيه المركب من الداخل والخارج ومن أفرده بالذكر أراد بالداخل والخارج المحض من ذلك، ثم البعض إنما يعرف الماهية إذا عرف شيئاً من أجزائها، إذ لو كانت الأجزاء بأسرها معلومة أو بقيت مجهولة لم يكن المعرف معرفاً أي سبباً لمعرفة الماهية وموصلاً إلى تصورها، فالجزء المعرف إن كان نفسه عاد المحذور. وإن كان غيره لزم التعريف بالخارج ضرورة كون كل جزء خارجاً عن الآخر، ولو فرض تداخلها بنقل الكلام إلى تعريفه للجزء منه ومن غيره، فيعود المحذور أو التعريف بالخارج وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الخارج إنما يفيد معرفة الماهية إذا علم اختصاصه بها بمعنى ثبوته لها ففيه عن جميع ما عداها وهذا تصديق يتوقف على تصور الماهية وهو دور، وتصور ما عداها من أمور الغير المتناهية على التفصيل وهو محال، وفي عبارة المواقف هنا تسامح حيث قال - والبعض إن عرف الماهية عرف نفسه وقد أبطل الخارج وسنبطله لأن الذي سيبطل هو التعريف بالخارج لا الخارج، وكأنه على حذف الباء أي عرف بالخارج أو على معنى أو عرف الأمر الذي شأنه أن يكون خارجاً عن سائر الأجزاء فيكون البعض المعرف خارجاً عنه ويلزم التعريف بالخارج لا الخارج، وإنما ادعى لزوم المحالين على ما هو تقرير المحصل بناء على أن معرف الماهية معرف لكل جزء منها، والظهور المنع عليه، اقتصرنا على أحدهما كما هو تقرير المطالب التالية، لا يخفى أن القدح في بعض مقدمات هذا الاستدلال كاف في دفعه إلا أنهم لما جوزوا التعريف بجميع الأجزاء بالبعض وبالخارج احتجنا إلى التقصي عن الإشكالات الثلاثة، أما عن الأول فبأن جميع الأجزاء وإن كانت نفس الماهية بالذات، لكن إنما يمتنع
As for the second: the acquirer—i.e., the definer (muʻarrif) of the quiddity—cannot be it itself, because it is impossible for a thing to be clearer and quicker in knowledge than itself. Rather, it is either all of its parts, which is itself, so the difficulty recurs; or some parts; or external to it. Included therein is the composite of internal and external, and whoever mentions them separately intends by 'internal' and 'external' the pure cases. Then, the part only defines the quiddity if it knows something of its parts, because if all parts were known or remained unknown, the definer would not be a definer—i.e., a cause for knowing the quiddity and a means to conceptualizing it. So if the defining part is itself, the difficulty recurs. If it is other than itself, then definition by what is external (taʻrīf bil-khārij) necessarily follows, since every part is external to another; even if one assumes their interpenetration, the discussion shifts to defining it by a part of it and of other things, so the difficulty recurs or definition by the external, which is also invalid—because the external only yields knowledge of the quiddity if its specificity to it is known, meaning its affirmation for it, which distinguishes it from all other things. This is an assent (taṣdīq) that depends on conceptualizing the quiddity—which is a circle—and conceptualizing all other things in detail, which is impossible. In the wording of al-Mawāqif there is leniency where it says: 'and if the part defines the quiddity, it defines itself; we have already refuted definition by the external and will refute it.' Because what will be refuted is definition by the external, not the external itself. It is as if the particle 'bi' (by) is omitted, meaning 'defines by the external', or else meaning 'defines the thing that is external to all parts, so the defining part is external to it and definition by the external follows, not the external.' He only claimed the necessity of two impossibilities according to the presentation of al-Muḥaṣṣal, based on the idea that the definer of the quiddity is a definer of each part of it—which is obviously denied. We confined ourselves to one of them as is the presentation of the subsequent inquiries. It is not hidden that refuting some premises of this argument suffices to refute it, but since they allowed definition by all parts, by some parts, and by the external, we needed to investigate the three objections. As for the first: because all parts, although they are the quiddity itself in essence, yet it is impossible...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion from the previous page, now focusing on the second point (وأما الثاني) regarding the definer (al-muʻarrif) of a quiddity (māhiyya). The author argues that the definer cannot be the quiddity itself because a thing cannot be clearer than itself. It must be either: (a) all of its parts—which is the same as itself, leading back to the same problem; (b) some parts; or (c) external to it. The composite of internal and external is also considered. Then a key argument: a part only defines the quiddity if it knows something of its parts. If all parts are known, the whole is already known, so no definition needed; if all parts are unknown, the part cannot provide knowledge. So the defining part either is itself (recurring problem) or other parts (leading to definition by the external, since each part is external to others). Definition by the external (taʻrīf bil-khārij) is invalid because it requires prior knowledge of the quiddity to know that the external property is specific to it—this is circular (dawr), and it would require conceptualizing all other distinct things in detail, which is impossible (infinite regress). The author then critiques a statement in al-Mawāqif, suggesting it is sloppy: it says 'if the part defines the quiddity, it defines itself' and claims to have refuted definition by the external; but the author clarifies that what is refuted is definition 'by' the external (using the particle bi), not the external itself. The passage suggests that al-Mawāqif meant 'defines by the external' or 'defines what is external'. Then the author notes that he could refute the whole argument by attacking a premise, but since opponents allow definition by all parts, some parts, and external, he must address three objections (al-ishkālāt al-thalāth). He begins with the first objection: that all parts are the quiddity itself in essence, yet it is impossible... (the text cuts off). The passage is a sophisticated logical analysis of the conditions for defining an essence, highlighting circularity and infinite regress, and engaging critically with earlier works like al-Mawāqif and al-Muḥaṣṣal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any part of an essence can define it without issue.
Correction: If the part is the whole, it's circular; if it's other than the whole, it leads to definition by external, which is invalid.

Checkpoint
Why is definition by the external (taʻrīf bil-khārij) considered invalid according to the passage?
A) Because the external is always unknown.
B) Because it requires prior knowledge of the quiddity, leading to circularity.
C) Because the external is not a part of the quiddity.
D) Because the external changes the quiddity.

Key Takeaway
The definer of a quiddity must be neither the same as it, nor its part, nor something external, because each leads to logical impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about yourself that is based on an external factor (e.g., your job, relationships, or achievements). According to this lesson, such a definition is logically flawed—it either circles back to itself or requires an impossible knowledge of all others. Can you see how this external dependence might be leading you away from a true understanding of yourself as a servant of Allah? What is one external label you will consciously detach from starting today?
?
The passage teaches that the definer cannot be the same as the defined. In your spiritual life, do you ever confuse your knowledge of Allah with Allah Himself? For instance, do you take pride in your Islamic knowledge as if it makes you superior? How does this logical principle humble you and remind you that your limited understanding is not the same as the Divine Reality? What practical step can you take to purify your intention in seeking knowledge?
